Preface
The research conducted as the foundation for this MSc Thesis has been carried out at the Depart-
ment of Health Technology at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) in cooperation with
NED University of Engineering and Technology, Pakistan, in partial fulfillment of the requirements
for the degree of Master of Science in Engineering.
The research presented in this thesis has been carried out over a period of five and a half months
while working as a student assistant engineer at Ambu. The thesis consists of a summary report
and developed algorithms. The workload corresponds to 32.5 ECTS.
This thesis would not have been possible without my main supervisor Sadasivan Puthusserypady
and co-supervisors Cihan Uyanik and Muhammad Ahmed Khan. I would like to thank them for
their contributions to this project and especially Cihan for his constructive feedback during our
weekly meetings. I would also like to thank Erla Hrafnkelsdóttir for our scientific discussions and
for providing support throughout this process as we have shared an oﬀice as well as the overall
project.
Finally, I would like to thank my boyfriend Tore Hilbert for all of the unconditional
support, knowledge and constructive feedback he has provided.
Kgs. Lyngby, July 2022

Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
iii
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In Danish:
For at stille diagnosen klinisk depression er det højest nødvendigt, at have en holistisk forståelse af
de fysiologiske processer, der er forbundet med depression. Dette gør den nuværende diagnostiske
praksis til påvisning af depression meget tidskrævende og involverer subjektive og inkonsistente
kliniske standarder, som ofte ikke klarer nøjagtigt at identificere og diagnosticere patienter uden
unødig forsinkelse. Derfor er en objektiv og non-invasiv tilgang til påvisning af depression øn-
skværdig, og vil muliggøre en pålidelig diagnose, rettidig intervention samt en vurdering af efter-
følgende behandlingsrespons.
I den forbindelse er en analyse af elektroencefalogrammet (EEG) blevet foreslået i litteraturen som
en objektiv og non-invasiv tilgang. På nuværende tidspunkt er automatisk påvisning af depression
baseret på EEG-signaler imidlertid afhængig af avancerede metoder og datasæt-specifikke præs-
tationer, som skal generaliseres og valideres på tværs af forskellige datasæt for at muliggøre en
vellykket implementering i det kliniske miljø.
Som et resultat heraf, foreslår denne afhandling et machine learning (ML) framework, der involverer
en analyse af uafhængige EEG-datasæt for at estimere generaliseringsevnen af tidligere anvendte
metoder og inkluderer en kombination af state-of-the-art statistiske, Fourier, wavelet og non-lineær
EEG-afledte features.
Forskellige feature selection metoder og classifiers blev undersøgt, samt
indflydelsen af modelparametre på klassificeringspræstationer.
De bedste ML modeller blev identificeret baseret på uafhængige datasæt, og efterfølgende blev de
vigtigste funktioner til analyse søgt valideret ved hjælp af et datasæt erhvervet efter behandling med
transcranial direct current stimulation (tDCS). Evaluering af modellerne blev udført på grundlag af
100 × 10-fold cross-validation, hvilket gav følgende metrics for performance: accuracy, sensitivity,
specificity, precision og F1-score.
Den bedste model præsenterede en gennemsnitlig accuracy på 95,60%, sensitivity på 90,91%, speci-
ficity på 99,29%, precision på 99,09% og F1-score på 94,81% baseret på et datasæt med 14 de-
primerede forsøgspersoner og 11 raske kontroller. Denne model leverede dog ikke en acceptabel og
robust ydeevne på tværs af datasæt.
Resultaterne af undersøgelsen bekræfter, at høje klassifikationspræstationer til påvisning af de-
pression kan opnås baseret på individuelle datasæt, men at modeller baseret på begrænsede under-
søgelsesdeltagere ikke er i stand til at generalisere mellem datasæt. Desuden præsenterede denne
afhandling nogle resultater for depressionsmarkører i EEG, hvorfra en konklusion er upålidelig. Det
er således nødvendigt med yderligere undersøgelser af EEG, for at vurdere resultaternes pålide-
lighed.
iv
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:6==
Abstract
In English:
For effective diagnosis of major depressive disorder (MDD) a holistic understanding of the phys-
iological processes associated with depression is of the utmost importance. This makes current
diagnostic practice for detection of depression highly time-consuming and involves subjective and
inconsistent clinical standards that often fail to accurately identify and diagnose individuals with-
out undue delay. Therefore, an objective and non-invasive approach for detection of depression is
highly desirable and would enable a reliable diagnosis, timely intervention as well as an evaluation
of subsequent treatment response.
In this regard, an analysis of the electroencephalogram (EEG) has been proposed in literature as
an objective and non-invasive approach. At the present time however, automatic identification
of depression based on EEG signals rely on advanced methodologies and dataset-specific perfor-
mances, which must be generalized and validated across different variations of data for successful
implementation into the clinical environment.
As a result, this thesis proposes a machine learning (ML) framework involving an analysis of
independent EEG datasets to estimate the generalization ability of previously applied methods
and includes a combination of state-of-the-art statistical, spectral, wavelet and non-linear EEG-
derived features. Various feature selection methods and classifiers are investigated as well as the
influence of model parameters on classification performances.
The best performing ML models were identified based on independent EEG datasets and after-
wards, the most important features for analysis were sought validated using a dataset acquired
after treatment with transcranial direct current stimulation (tDCS). The evaluation of the models
was conducted based on 100 iterations of 10-fold cross-validation, providing performance metrics
including accuracy, sensitivity, specificity, precision and F1-score.
The best performing model presented an average accuracy of 95.60%, sensitivity of 90.91%, speci-
ficity of 99.29%, precision of 99.09% and F1-score of 94.81% based on a dataset of 14 depressed
subjects and 11 healthy controls. However, this model did not provide an acceptable and robust
performance across datasets and warrants a larger study to determine the clinical utility of the
state-of-the-art methods applied.
The results of the study confirm that high classification performances for identification of depression
is achievable based on individual dataset but that models based on limited study participants are
unable to generalize well between datasets. Furthermore, the study presented some results with
regard to the findings of depression biomarkers from which an evaluation is unreliable. Therefore,
further studies of depression are required to evaluate the reliability of these.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Clinical depression, also known as major depressive disorder (MDD), is an affective psychiatric
disorder estimated to affect 3.8% of the world’s population [1]. When clinical depression is severe
or recurrent, it may become a serious health condition and is characterized by symptoms includ-
ing sadness, feelings of guilt and low self-esteem or self-worth. According to the World Health
Organization, depression takes the lives of 700 000 people annually, making it the fourth leading
cause of death among 15 to 29-year-olds [1]. Although effective treatments for clinical depres-
sion are publicly known, more than 75% of people in low- and middle-income countries receive
no treatment. This barrier may be partially explained by the lack of resources, lack of trained
health-care professionals, the nature of the disease, as well as the social stigma associated with
psychiatric disorders. In countries of all income levels, psychiatry has no objective tests or criteria
for the disease, which often means that people affected by depression are not diagnosed accurately
or prescribed antidepressants when in fact they do not have the disorder.
Considering these aspects, there is certainly room for improvement of how we define, detect and
treat depression. Nowadays, the most commonly used diagnosis of depression is a scale-based inter-
view conducted by a general practitioner, psychologist or psychiatrist. The criteria for depression
listed in the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders (DSM-5) is the current inter-
national standard as well as the clinical test Mini-Mental State Examination (MMSE) for late-life
depression [2]. Other conventional psychometric questionnaires such as the Beck depression in-
ventory (BDI) and Hamilton Depression Rating Scale (HDRS) are used for screening rather than
performing the actual diagnosis.
With the advent of more sophisticated technological solutions for understanding the complex mech-
anisms of depression, more and more ongoing research in neurological science is directed to finding
convenient and effective methods for detection of depression. Specifically, advances in sensor and
mobile technology have created an exploratory incentive for using physiological data as an objective
and accurate tool [3].
Of the various types of physiological data, the electroencephalogram (EEG) is a measure of the
electrical activity of the brain and is known to reflect its dynamic cognitive state in real time. EEG
signals are spontaneous electrical fluctuations resulting from ionic currents along the neurons of
the brain and recorded from multiple electrodes attached to the scalp surface. Since the electrical
phenomena of the brain was first discovered in 1875 as well as the first human EEG in 1924, the
interpretation of the association between brain function and psychological disorders have been of
immense study. Research conducted within the fields of neuroscience, psychology, and cognitive
science have shown that it is possible to indicate the majority of psychological activities as well
as emotional states using the complex signals of the brain and could likewise reflect emotional
transformation [2].
Major contributors to the underpinnings of depression and similar psychiatric disorders are vari-
ous neuroimaging techniques and methods such as functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI),
MRI, digital tractography imaging (DTI), functional anisotropy (FA) and magnetoencephalogra-
phy (MEG). However, the advantages of EEG signals are significant and include a higher time
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
1
==PAGE:13==
resolution, lower cost and simpler operation method, which may enable an opportunity of earlier
detection and timely intervention. Thus, the analysis of EEG has been proposed as an objec-
tive and non-invasive approach to study cognitive behavior, mental disorders and other illnesses
including epilepsy, sleep disorders, and depression.
From previously published literature, including fMRI, DTI (including FA) and graph theory stud-
ies, it seems that major depressive disorder disrupts the global topological organisations of the brain
networks [3]. Vederine et al. and Kwaasteniet et al. elaborated on this and found an abnormal
functional connectivity in the fronto-limbic system [3]. This notion has since then been reflected
in EEG literature as this abnormality possibly affects the excitability of the cortex allowing for
detection of depression using the well established EEG [3][4].
In recent years, transcranial direct current stimulation (tDCS) has emerged as a promising safe
and non-invasive neurostimulation method for modulating cortical activity and treating various
psychiatric disorders such as depression. However, the neurophysiological mechanisms involved in
the antidepressant effects of tDCS remain incompletely understood and has yet to be validated
and regulated by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration (FDA) as an effective and safe treatment
[5] [6].
tDCS is applied by inducing a constant, low direct current to specific areas of the brain via
electrodes attached to the scalp.
The current induces an intracerebral current flow, which in
turn increases/decreases the neuronal excitability based on the type of stimulation being used. In
2016, Al-Kayasi et al. [7] aimed at investigating the feasibility of identifying depressed patients
responding to tDCS treatment based on resting-state EEG. This finding demonstrates that an
automatic detection of depression and subsequent identification of tDCS treatment response is
achievable and may be used to validate the eﬀicacy of tDCS therapy as well as predict the pace of
recovery or treatment output.
However, as present literature describes ways to identify depression and its potential treatment
response, there is still much to be done in terms of generalization and reproducibility to apply these
methods in clinical settings. The variability in terms of methodologies used constitutes a funda-
mental problem making them challenging to compare as well as determining their robustness and
ability to maintain performance with different variations of data. In present literature, the vari-
ability among EEG channels and montages is considerable along with the size of the dataset, the
number of electrodes, the sampling frequency, artifact removal schemes, linear or nonlinear mea-
sures calculated from the raw signal, whether they used specific frequency bands or the broadband
signal for analysis, what machine learning methods they used and finally, the obtained classification
accuracy [3].
Thus, automatic algorithms must be built to maintain performance with different variations of
data and must be reproducible to help generalize current methods for identification of depression.
Building such algorithms will be the central focus of this thesis, as these applied to EEG signals
recorded before and after the application of tDCS would help provide a standard for the imple-
mentation into clinical settings as well as provide a fast, precise and objective identification of
depression, therapy eﬀicacy coupled with the pace of recovery or treatment output. Moreover,
such automatic algorithms may potentially reveal hidden signal characteristics and identify new
neuromarkers, thus providing a better characterization of depression.
1.1
Problem Statement
Current diagnostic practice involves time-consuming subjective, inconsistent, and imprecise clinical
standards making an objective and automatic identification of individuals suffering from depression
of utmost importance. In literature, the automatic identification of depression and subsequent
treatment response rely on various advanced methodologies and in this context, the performance
of algorithms vary a lot and must be generalized and validated across different variations of data
for a successful implementation into the clinical environment. Furthermore, an objective detection
of depression and subsequent identification of treatment response may be used to validate the
eﬀicacy of therapy as well as predict the pace of recovery and/or treatment output.

1.2
Research Questions and Objectives
Considering the motivation of the introduction and problem statement, the following research
questions and objectives were defined as follows
Question 1: Which one of the available automatic methods is optimal for identification of clinical
depression and subsequent treatment response?
Automatic methods for identification of clinical depression and/or treatment response have
already been proposed. However, recognition of future research directions and generalized
methods achieving the highest performance in identifying patients with depression and treat-
ment response is necessary and must be identified for successful implementation into the
clinical environment.
Question 2: How does variability in terms of methodologies affect proposed state-of-the-art ma-
chine learning methods for identification of clinical depression?
As methodological variability and inconsistencies are present within automatic identification
of clinical depression, it is still unknown whether such methods based on EEG signal pro-
cessing and machine learning may be able to accurately identify clinical depression based
on different variations of data. Thus, the variability of research methods must be investi-
gated and how these methods affect classification performances for robust identification of
depression.
Question 3: Can a novel automatic method overcome the current state-of-the-art machine learn-
ing methods for identifying clinical depression?
At the present time, it is diﬀicult to accurately compare the performance of scientific litera-
ture and determine the eﬀicacy of state-of-the-art machine learning methods for automatic
identification of depression. This is due to several inconsistencies present with regard to
the methodology of the applied algorithms and limited number of study participants in the
datasets of investigation. A novel and robust machine learning method must be developed
to overcome these inconsistencies and help establish future research directions.
1.3
Thesis Outline
The research content of the thesis is structured into 8 chapters, necessary for addressing the 3
research questions. Besides these chapters, the thesis contains this introduction, a physiological
background and a conclusive chapter outlining the findings of this thesis.
Chapter 3 includes the formal comparison of scientific literature available for detection of de-
pression and outlines the findings of depression biomarkers.
Chapter 4 presents a preliminary data overview and includes an introduction to data acquisition
and the study participants of investigation.
Chapter 5 outlines the theoretical background of the wavelet transform, which is a mathematical
tool of the proposed algorithm.
Chapter 6 provides a detailed description of the final algorithm developed in this project. In
particular, the theoretical background and reasoning behind the proposed methodology is
presented.
Chapter 7 presents the outcomes of the methodology and evaluates the performances of the
proposed algorithms using a secondary dataset.
Chapter 8 interprets and discusses the principal findings of the thesis and introduces additional
research directions.

Chapter 2
Physiological Background
This chapter aims to briefly introduce the electrical properties of the brain and how this is altered
in patients suffering from depression. Initally, the structure of the brain and neural oscillations
contributing to the recording of electrical activity will be explained. Afterwards, the physiolog-
ical foundation of the electroencephalogram will be explained, including an overview of common
electrode locations and montages used for analysis. At last, the nature of depression, the current
challenges in identification and characterization of depression, as well as treatment strategies will
be presented to outline the importance of this work and ease the reading of the rest of the thesis.
2.1
Electrical Properties of the Brain
The brain is composed of numerous complex geometrical structures operating to control every
process that regulates the human body. The cerebral cortex is the outermost layer of the brain
covering the surfaces of the cerebral hemispheres and is folded into ridges (gyri), and folds (sulci).

Figure 2.1: The anatomy of the human brain and major subdivisions of the cerebral cortex [8].
Each cerebral hemisphere controls the opposite part of the body and may be divided into four
lobes: the frontal, parietal, temporal and occipital lobe, each controlling specific functions. An
overview of basic brain anatomy and subdivisions of the cerebral cortex is presented in Figure 2.1.
The lobes of the cerebral cortex consists of grey matter and is primarily composed of nerve cell
bodies and dendrites whose primary function is to receive synaptic signals. Underneath the cortical
tissues lie the white matter and contains the nerve fibers (axons) that connect nerve cells (neurons)
by carrying electrical impulses (called action potentials) and releasing chemical signalling molecules
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
5
==PAGE:17==
(neurotransmitters). The general structure of neurons is presented in Figure 2.2. As visualised,
the axons are typically surrounded by myelin (a lipid-rich sheath) to isolate them and increase the
rate of propagation. Action potentials are used to carry information from one part of the cell to
another and functions by initiating large and rapid reversible fluctuations in electrical voltage across
the cellular membrane (called the membrane potential). This happens through voltage-gated ion
channels embedded in the cellular membrane and located at regularly spaced uninsulated patches
(called nodes of Ranvier). At the furthest end of the axon, the cell branches into several axon
terminals that contain specific signalling molecules (neurotransmitters) enclosed in membrane-
bound spheres (called synaptic vesicles). The release of neurotransmitters is regulated by voltage-
dependent calcium channels and enables the interaction with receptors on other neurons or non-
neuronal cells such as muscle cells or glands. The effect of the neurotransmitters are dependant
on the receptors on the target cell where binding may cause excitation, inhibition, or modulation
of connecting nerve cells.
Depending on the type of neuron, typically identified according to where they originate from,
project to and which neurotransmitters they use, they often exhibit a characteristic pattern of firing.
Some neurons are primarily resting (at the resting membrane potential) and may be triggered by
an external stimulus while others fire action potentials in a repetitive pattern without the need of
external stimulus. In this way, the electrical properties of neurons are modulated by contributions
from the environment, hormones or chemical and electrical signals transmitted from neighboring
neurons [9].
A detailed description of the phases of the action potential has been included in
Appendix A.1.

2.1.1
Neural Oscillations
Neuronal oscillations are rhythmic or repetitive patterns of neural activity controlled by individual
neurons or their interactions.
Oscillations of individual neurons may appear as the rhythmic
patterns of action potentials, which then activate post-synaptic neurons or appear as fluctuations
in the membrane potential across the intracellular and extracellular volumes. Sometimes individual
neurons interact and produce synchronised activity which may give rise to large-scale oscillations,
also visualised as brainwaves in the electroencephalogram (EEG). These interactions generally
arise from positive feedback mechanisms and may give rise to oscillations at different frequencies
than the firing pattern of individual neurons. An example of such feedback mechanism is seen
between the thalamus located in the central part of the brain and the cortex. This is called the
thalamocortical network and is believed to arise from the time delay and structure of the neural
connections between the regions. Thus, the propagation delay and partial synchronization between
subsets of brain areas oscillating at different frequencies may cause a variety of oscillatory activity.
Oscillatory activity have been linked to many cognitive functions and may be characterized by
frequency, amplitude and phase [11].
Neural activity is typically studied in terms of macroscopic oscillations and include a broad range of
6
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:18==
spectral content that may be divided into specific frequency bands according to different cognitive
characteristics. The best-known frequency band is the alpha activity (8−12Hz) which is prominent
during relaxed wakefulness and when the eyes are closed. Other commonly accepted frequency
bands are the delta (1–4 Hz), theta (4–8 Hz), beta (13–30 Hz), low gamma (30–70 Hz),[13] and
high gamma (70–150 Hz) frequency bands. An overview of the these frequency bands as well as
their cognitive characteristics have been included in Table 2.1.
Table 2.1: Frequency bands of macroscopic neural oscillations as well as their cognitive character-
istics typically measured using the EEG.

2.1.2
The Electroencephalogram
The electroencephalogram (EEG) is a non-invasive recording of the electrical activity of the brain.
More specifically, electrodes on the scalp surface record local field potentials (LFPs) from post-
synaptic activity of synchronously activated neurons (see Figure A.2 in Appendix A.2). To reach
the electrodes on the scalp, LFPs generated in the cortex must pass several meninges, cerebrospinal
fluid (CSF) and tissues including blood vessels with different electrical properties and geometry.
Thus, the LFPs are attenuated and distorted throughout the tissues from the cortex influencing
the precision and spatial resolution of the EEG. Nevertheless, LFPs form the building blocks of
EEG and may be used to asses the underlying activity of the brain and potential neurological
disorders. Please refer to Appendix A.2 for a description of pyramidal neurons and how these
affect the LFPs recorded using an EEG device.
2.1.2.1
Electrode locations
In conventional and non-invasive EEG, the electrodes are attached to the scalp with a conductive
gel to reduce electrical impedance. The locations of the electrodes are typically chosen according
to the international 10-20 system enabling studies of EEG to be compared and effectively analyzed.
Figure 2.3: The placement of electrodes according to the international 10–20 system [12].
As visualized in Figure 2.3, the 10-20 system is based on the distances (10% or 20% of the total
front–back or right–left distance) between adjacent electrodes on the scalp and the location of
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
7
==PAGE:19==
the underlying areas of the cerebral cortex (pre-frontal (Fp), frontal (F), temporal (T), parietal
(P), occipital (O), and central (C)). Note that there is no central lobe, but is merely used for
identification purposes. Besides being given a letter to identify the lobe, a number is additionally
given to identify the hemisphere location and which electrode is closer to the midline. Even numbers
(2, 4, 6, 8) refer to the right hemisphere and odd numbers (1, 3, 5, 7) refer to the left hemisphere.
To identify the electrodes on the midline, these are referred to with a ’z’. Cz and Fz are typically
the ’common’ or ’ground’ electrodes and the A1 and A2 electrodes (also called M1 and M2) are
placed on the auricle of the ear for referential montages [13][14]. In this thesis, the placement of
electrodes will be based on the international 10–20 system and an extended system known as the
international 10-10 system, where additional electrodes are placed between existing ones.
2.1.2.2
Montages
To produce each trace of activity or channel in the EEG, the voltage difference between two
electrodes is computed using a differential amplifier. The amplitude is typically in the order of 10
to 100 µV in an adult EEG. The representation of EEG channels, depending on which pairs of
electrodes are connected to which differential amplifier is called a montage and may include one of
three standard recording derivations: common reference, average reference or bipolar (see Figure
2.4).
A.
B.
C.
Figure 2.4: EEG montage maps presenting examples of (A.) the average montage, (B.) linked-ears
montage and (C.) bipolar montage [15].
Referential montages compare individual electrodes to a single reference. This reference may be a
number of things, but most commonly, individual electrodes are compared to the average voltage
of all electrodes (termed the average reference montage) or the auricle(s) of the ear, which serves as
a common reference montage or ’linked ears’ montage. The ’linked ears’ montage will specifically
be investigated in this thesis but will be compared to related literature employing other montages.
Bipolar montages represent individual channels as the voltage difference between two adjacent
electrodes. There are multiple types of bipolar montages, but typically an electrode’s voltage is
compared to the voltage of the electrode behind it forming a chain of electrodes from the front to
the back of the head or transversely across the head.
Although international standards have been made for the placement of electrodes on the scalp with
regard to EEG recordings such as the 10-20 system, the variability among montages is considerable
and may affect the performance of algorithms. In particular, due to the highly nonlinear and noisy
process of recording these signals, the choice of reference plays a significant role in the final quality
and must be considered prior to studies evaluating clinical decision-making, as impure grounding
and choice of reference could falsify measurements [16][17].
2.2
Understanding Clinical Depression
Clinical depression is an illness of the brain characterized by symptoms of persistent sadness,
irritability, emptiness or loss of energy and pleasure in activities for most of the day, nearly every
day, for at least two weeks. Clinical depression affects people irrespective of age, race, ethnicity, and
8
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:20==
gender, and affected individuals describe several other symptoms such as feelings of varying degrees
of helplessness, poor concentration, feelings of excessive guilt and low self-worth, hopelessness for
the future, thoughts-, plans- or attempts of death or suicide, disrupted sleep and changes in appetite
or weight. Depression can be recurrent and long-lasting and may result in a significant impairment
of the ability to function in most aspects of life with adverse effects on physical health, human
relationships and cognitive function [1].
A distinction is made between mild, moderate, or severe depression depending on the severity of
the symptoms and impact on the individual’s ability to function. Different patterns of depres-
sion include single episode depressive disorder, recurrent depressive disorder, and bipolar disorder
characterized by episodes of mania, hypomania (mild form of mania), depression or mixed state.
Clinical depression, or major depressive disorder (MDD), is the most investigated type of depression
and evidenced by one or more major depressive episodes.
2.2.1
Effects of Depression
The effects of depression are pronounced and leads to a severe reduction in quality of life as
well as an increased risk of suicide and long-term consequences. These consequences may present
themselves in the form of permanent cognitive disorders and permanent loss of both functional
and occupational ability. With consequent suffering for the individual along with socio-economic
consequences, depression is a major contributor to the overall global burden of disease and is a
leading cause of disability worldwide [1] [18].
The socio-economic effects of depression are significant with indirect and direct consequences for
the workplace evident by reduced productivity and close to 20% of lost work days in total [19].
Depressed individuals are also five times more likely to become unemployed, thus imposing further
significant costs on the individual, the community and the economy at large [20][21].
2.2.2
Pathophysiology of Depression
Despite significant research conducted within the fields of neuroscience, psychology, and cognitive
science, the understanding of the precise mechanisms by which depression develops as well as the
pathophysiology of depression remain incomplete. This is partially attributable to the heteroge-
neous nature of the disease and its potential etiologies. At the present time, factors of genetic,
environmental, endocrine, immunologic factors, and neurogenesis may all be linked to the patho-
physiology of depression.
A holistic understanding of these physiological processes that cause,
result from, or are otherwise associated with depression is of the utmost importance for effective
diagnosis and treatment.
Most likely, depression involves various complex and interconnected
mechanisms which manifest as the signs and symptoms interpreted as depression [19]. A thorough
description of the mechanisms by which depression develops as well as the factors linked to the
pathophysiology of depression is included in Appendix A.3.
2.2.3
Diagnosis of Depression
At the present time, a diagnosis of major depressive disorder may be based on 1) a physical
examination to evaluate if the affected person suffers from an underlying physical health problem,
2) lab tests to examine the complete blood count or thyroid function, 3) a psychiatric evaluation
that may include a questionnaire of specific symptoms, thoughts, feelings and behavioral patterns
or 4) an evaluation based on the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders (DSM-5)
outlining specific underlying criteria for depression. The latter, constitutes the current clinical
standard and provides a common ground for clinicians and researchers to establish a diagnosis as
well as assess the effectiveness of treatments. [2][22][23].
2.2.4
Treatment of Depression
Depending on the severity of the symptoms and impact on the ability to function over time,
mental health professionals may offer psychological treatments such as behavioural activation,
cognitive behavioural therapy and interpersonal psychotherapy, and/or antidepressant medication
targeting neurotransmitter pathways ie. for increased concentrations and/or uptake in the synaptic
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
9
==PAGE:21==
cleft. Antidepressants are usually not the first line of treatment for mild depression and health
professionals must consider possible adverse effects [1]. Other less typical treatments of depression
include brain stimulation therapies such as electroconvulsive therapy and transcranial magnetic
stimulation and vagus nerve stimulation. A brief description of the procedure and effect of these
brain stimulation therapies has been included in Appendix A.4.
Besides these neurostimulation therapies, transcranial direct current stimulation (tDCS) has emerged
as a promising non-invasive method for modulating cortical activity in depression. For several years,
tDCS has been used experimentally in smaller and larger trials for its antidepressant effects, yet
clinical use of tDCS remains limited, partly due to an incomplete understanding of its underlying
neurophysiological mechanisms and furthermore, because the FDA has not yet issued an opinion
of its eﬀicacy [24].
tDCS is portable, cost-effective and relatively free of side effects and it works by delivering a con-
stant low direct current (anodal or cathodal) via electrodes attached to the scalp. The current
alters neuronal excitability either by causing the resting membrane potential of neurons to depo-
larize (anodal tDCS) or hyperpolarize (cathodal tDCS). A depolarization of the neurons promotes
more spontaneous cell firing, whereas a hyperpolarization decreases spontaneous cell firing. The
lasting effect of the stimulation has been shown to depend on the duration as well as the intensity
of the current, but must remain a topic of investigation for successful implementation into clinical
settings. The stimulation has additionally been shown to promote a persistent strengthening of
synapses in the brain and long term depression, but must likewise be validated in supplemental
studies. In the case of depression, tDCS specifically targets the left side of prefrontal cortex, as
this area of the brain has been associated with reduced neuronal activity [25].

Chapter 3
Related Work
This chapter provides a brief and yet complete introduction to the examined articles of this work
to acquaint the reader with previous work conducted in the regard of more accurate identification
of depression based on resting-state EEG. The first of three sections describes related studies for
detection of depression and particularly studies inspiring the automatic algorithm presented in
this thesis. The second section presents an overview of related depression biomarkers and the final
section presents the proposed approach of this work.
3.1
Automatic Identification of Depression
In literature, a number of methods have been proposed for automatic identification of depression.
In this work, the focus has been utilizing machine learning algorithms to detect depression based on
EEG signals. The selection process of primary papers was carried out by applying some principles
and inclusion/exclusion criteria. Inclusion criteria are formulated as containing studies which have
been published between 2012 and 2021 and which have focused on adopting conventional machine
learning methods to detect or predict depression using non-invasive resting-state scalp EEG signals.
Exclusion criteria are contained studies which have not been written in English, have not reached
a minimum of 10 citations or does not contain a modest number of study participants (<20).
Furthermore, articles related to predicting depression and/or treatment response or processing
brain signals for detection of other brain disorders were continuously examined throughout the
thesis by considering the same criteria.
DTU Findit, Science Direct, and IEEE Explorer was
primarily used as scientific electronic databases. A complete presentation of all the publications
considered throughout this project is not the main intention of this chapter and therefore only
literature of specific relevance to the thesis will be presented in detail.
The research questions of this work will be investigated by considering the variety of agendas
presented in previous work. This includes investigating the performance of spectral features derived
from Fourier and wavelet analysis (Mumtaz et al. [26][27]), investigating how spectral features
compare with complex and non-linear features (Hosseinifard et al.
[28]) and the methodology
behind some of the best performances described in present literature (Faust et al. [4] and Movahed
et al. [29]). The publications are presented one-by-one and include a brief introduction, an overview
of applied methodology, results and key findings. The majority of publications present the results of
their validation by specifying the accuracy as well as sensitivity and specificity presented in Section
6.4.3. As presented in Section 6.4.3, the accuracy is a measure for the fraction of total predictions
the model got right. This measure may however be misleading by itself, if the distribution of classes
is imbalanced. The sensitivity is also used to evaluate model performance because it is a measure
of how well the model detects positive cases (in this case depressed cases). Likewise, specificity
is used as a measure for determining the proportion of actual negatives, which got predicted as
negative (in this case healthy cases).
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
11
==PAGE:23==
Electroencephalogram (EEG)-based Computer-aided Technique to Diagnose Major
Depressive Disorder (MDD)
Biomedical Signal Processing and Control, 2017 - Mumtaz et al. [26]
Introduction: This work proposes EEG signal power and EEG alpha inter-hemispheric asym-
metry from the frontal, temporal, parietal and occipital brain areas as suitable clinical diagnostic
tools for detecting major depressive disorder.
Methods: Using 5 minutes of both eyes-open and eyes-closed data involving a 19-channel EEG
cap with linked-ear (LE) reference, the data of 63 participants (33 MDD & 30 HC) was initially
high- and low-pass filtered according to the frequencies of the brain using a cut-off frequency of
0.5 Hz and 70 Hz, respectively. A notch filter was applied to remove powerline interference and
the data was re-referenced to an infinity reference (IR), as proposed by Qin et al.[30] to avoid a
potential bias. Other artefacts originating from eye blinks, movement or muscular activities were
removed using a standard brain electric source analysis (BESA) software. For feature extraction, 2
minutes of artefact free eyes-open (EO) and eyes-closed (EC) data was used to compute the power
at four different frequency bands (using Welch’s method for spectral density estimation) and EEG
alpha asymmetry for each channel pair. To enhance the performance of the algorithm and reduce
redundancy, the features were ranked according to a weight (z-value) using the receiver operating
characteristics (ROC). The top ranked 5, 10, 15, and 19 features were used in an iterative manner
during classification. 10-fold cross-validation was repeated 100 times to ensure the validity of the
testing results using logistic regression (LR), naïve bayes (NB) and support vector machine (SVM)
using a linear kernel.
Results: The algorithm was implemented on data published by Mumtaz et al. [27] where SVM
classification using a linear kernel achieved the highest average accuracy of 98.4% with sensitivity
and specificity of 96.7% and 100%, respectively. These results were obtained using 19 features of
inter-hemispheric asymmetry but similar results were obtained using a combination of power and
asymmetry features.
Key findings: The study found that depressed participants tend to exhibit greater right frontal
alpha activity compared to healthy controls. Furthermore, it was found that EEG signal power
is lower for depressed participants in the theta and alpha bands in all brain regions compared
to healthy controls. These findings are supported by other works but diverging results exist and
more studies containing additional study participants are needed for a better understanding of the
specific physiological differences and impact of these frequencies in depression [31].
A Wavelet-based Technique to Predict Treatment Outcome for Major Depressive
Disorder
PLoS ONE, 2017 - Mumtaz et al. [27]
Introduction: This work proposes a wavelet-based technique based on features extracted from
EEG data involving delta and theta frequency bands for predicting depression and treatment
response.
Methods: Using EEG and ERP data acquired from a cap of 19 electrodes according to the
linked-ears reference, the data of 64 participants (34 MDD and 30 HC) was re-referenced to the
infinity reference and filtered from 0.1 to 70 Hz with an additional 50 Hz notch filter to suppress
powerline noise. EEG and ERP data was recorded pre-treatment (before start of antidepressants)
and every week until completion of the study lasting 6 weeks in total. This thesis only considers the
methodology concerning the resting state EEG data including 5 minutes of open-eyes and closed-
eyes data. Artifact corrections were initially performed with semi-automatic filtering techniques
implemented in the brain electrical source analysis (BESA) software corresponding to eye blinks,
muscle activity, powerline noise and heart activity. Wavelet analysis using the Daubechies 4 (Db4)
window function was performed using segments of 2 minutes. Resulting wavelet coeﬀicients from
delta (A4) and theta (D4) bands were more eﬀicient (in terms of accuracy) than other bands.
12
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:24==
Hence, these bands were selected for building classification models. Short-time Fourier transform
(STFT) and empirical mode decomposition (EMD) were also applied for comparison to wavelet
analysis.
The dimensionality of the feature matrices were reduced using a rank-based feature
selection method according to the receiver operating characteristics (ROC) criterion. The minimum
redundancy maximum relevance (mRMR) method was used to compare the rank-based feature
selection method with a standard method.
Finally, the extracted features were used as input
data to train and test a logistic regression (LR) classifier involving 100 iterations of 10-fold cross-
validation.
Results: Using delta and theta wavelet coeﬀicients, the best accuracy of 90.5 ± 8.3% was obtained
using wavelet coeﬀicients in combination with STFT and EMD, with sensitivity and specificity of
91.6 ± 5.7% and 88.7 ± 7.5%, respectively. The method proposed for automatic identification of
depression however, was based on wavelet analysis and included an accuracy of 89.6±5.1 wit a
sensitivity of 81.7±11.3% and specificity of 96.7±3.1%.
Key findings: As commonly suggested by related literature, the authors found that frontal and
temporal brain regions are better at distinguishing between depressed and healthy cases [32][2].
This study also found that using segment lengths of two minutes achieve slightly better accuracy
results compared to data lengths of one and three minutes. This finding contradicts other related
works and must be validated using bigger sample sizes.
Furthermore, the authors found that
compared to mRMR, rank-based feature selection achieves better classification results.
It was
also indicated that EEG features from wavelet analysis shows higher classification accuracies than
STFT and EMD. It is however important to consider that these findings should be generalized by
additional studies as related articles have not compared these particular feature selection methods
as well as investigated the use of EMD for detection of depression.
Classifying Depression Patients and Normal Subjects using Machine Learning
Techniques and Nonlinear Features from EEG Signal
Computer Methods and Programs in Biomedicine, 2013 - Hosseinifard et al. [28]
Introduction: This study compares linear and non-linear analysis and presents non-linear analysis
as most effective for discriminating depressed patients and healthy controls.
Methods: EEG signals were high- and low-pass filtered with a 0.5 Hz and 70 Hz cutoff frequency,
respectively. A notch filter was used to remove powerline interference at 50 Hz and any remain-
ing EEG artifacts were visually inspected and discarded.
Afterwards, the power of four EEG
frequency bands (using Welch’s method) and four non-linear features, including correlation dimen-
sion, Higuchi’s fractal dimension, detrended fluctuation analysis (DFA) and largest lyapunov ex-
ponent (LLE) were calculated. Feature selection was performed using the Genetic algorithm (GA)
to find significant features and improve the performance of classification. By applying leave-one-
out cross-validation for training and testing, three classification techniques are presented including
LDA, LR and k-NN. Other classification techniques such as SVM with a non-linear kernel and NB
were also tested but were found inferior despite trying different combinations of features.
Results: Using a private dataset of 45 depressive patients and 45 normal subjects, the highest
classification accuracy of 90% was achieved using all non-linear features and the LR classifier.
Other performance metrics such as sensitivity and specificity were not published.
Key findings: As found by related work, this study suggests that depressed patients differ in the
alpha band more than other bands, especially in the left hemisphere compared to healthy controls.
The study also indicate that non-linear features are effective methods for analyzing EEG signals.
These findings have likewise been established by related literature. By considering individual non-
linear features, the highest accuracy was achieved using the correlation dimension in contrast to
the LLE providing the lowest accuracy across all classifiers. It was also found that the Genetic
algorithm performs better than PCA for feature selection and significantly increases classification
accuracy. These methods have not been compared by related literature and must be investigated
for future research directions.
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
13
==PAGE:25==
Depression Diagnosis Support System based on EEG Signal Entropies
Journal of Mechanics in Medicine and Biology, 2014 - Faust et al. [4]
Introduction: This work proposes a successful EEG processing structure that combines wavelet
packet decomposition (WPD) and non-linear algorithms for diagnosing and monitoring the treat-
ment of patients suffering from depression.
Methods: Bipolar resting-state EEG recordings from electrode locations FP1-T3 (left half) and
FP2-T4 (right half) were recorded for 5 minutes. The data was notch filtered to remove power
line interference at 50 Hz, while high frequency components were removed using a total variation
filtering algorithm. Information of the specific frequency band was not provided by the authors.
The features were extracted using a 2-level WPD with the Daubechies 8 (Db8) window function.
The resulting subband signals were used as input for non-linear algorithms including bispectral
entropy, Renyi’s entropy, approximate entropy and sample entropy. The significance of the features
were determined based on statistical analysis using the students t-test. Afterwards, classification
was conducted using seven different classification algorithms, including: Gaussian Mixture Model
(GMM), DT, k-NN, SVM, NB, probabilistic neural network (PNN) and Fuzzy Soft‐max Classifier
(FSC). The classifiers were evaluated based on 10-fold stratified cross validation, where the MDD
and HC were randomly divided into homogeneous subgroups.
Results: The study obtained a classification accuracy of 99.5%, sensitivity of 99.2% and specificity
of 99.7% using a PNN classifier to differentiate 30 normal and 30 depressed participants.
Key findings: As reported by many studies, the authors report that EEG signals from the left
and the right brain regions are different. They also found that signals from the right part of the
brain discriminate better between depressed and healthy subjects and that the variability in the
signal according to entropy measures is less for depressed subjects. Entropy measures have become
an established and common measure to distinguish between the two classes but findings should be
generalized by using bigger sample sizes for consistent results.
A Major Depressive Disorder Classification Framework based on EEG Signals using
Statistical, Spectral, Wavelet, Functional Connectivity, and Nonlinear Analysis
Journal of Neuroscience Methods, 2021 - Movahed et al. [29]
Introduction: This work proposes a machine learning framework for MDD diagnosis and considers
a combination of EEG-derived features extracted using statistical, spectral, wavelet, functional
connectivity, and nonlinear analysis methods.
Methods: The signals were initially high- and low-pass filtered with a 0.5 Hz and 32 Hz cutoff
frequency, respectively. To remove other artifacts, independent artifact components were removed
using independent component analysis (ICA) as well as manual inspection of components in time
and frequency domains. The filtered signals were visually inspected to eliminate remaining noisy
intervals. After eliminating all noisy intervals, eyes-open and eyes-closed signals were segmented
into samples of 1 minute (249 MDD samples and 261 HC samples) each containing 19 electrode
channels. Statistical features were extracted of the segments and include average, skewness, kur-
tosis, minimum, maximum, and Hjorth parameters (activity, complexity, mobility). Likewise, the
proposed method employed the signal band power in four common frequency bands using Welch’s
method for spectral density estimation as well as inter-hemispheric asymmetry as spectral features.
Wavelet energy was estimated using a three-level discrete wavelet transform decomposition and
conducted using the Coiflet 5 (Coif5) window function. Based on the assumption that EEG signals
are non-stationary and stochastic, containing some nonlinear characteristics, non-linear methods
such as detrended fluctuation analysis, Higuchi, correlation dimension, Lyapunov exponent, C0-
complexity, Kolmogorov entropy, Shannon entropy, and approximate entropy were applied. At
last, functional connectivity analysis for analyzing the dynamic coordination of neuronal activities
in the brain was conducted based on the synchronization likelihood between different channels. To
reduce the dimension of feature space, and improve the performance of classification, sequential
14
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:26==
backward feature selection (SBFS) was employed prior to 10-fold cross-validation using classifiers
such as SVM with linear (LINSVM) and radial basis function (RBFSVM) kernels, LR, decision
trees (DT), NB, and some ensemble classifiers like RusBoost (RB), GentleBoost (GB), and Random
Forest (RF).
Results: The proposed method was implemented on a dataset provided by Mumtaz et al. ([27])
and validated using the MODMA dataset containing 24 MDD and 29 HC subjects.[33] On the
dataset provided by Mumtaz et al., the algorithm outperformed other approaches of classification
based on EEG signals and achieved the highest accuracy of 99.0% with sensitivity of 98.4% and
specificity of 99.6% based on the RBFSVM classifier. When applied on the MODMA dataset,
LINSVM provided the best classification performance and obtained an average accuracy of 97.3%
with sensitivity of 98.3% and specificity of 97.0%.
Key findings: In contrast to Mumtaz et al. [27], the authors found that segmenting the signals
into 1 minute segments led to better classification performance compared to using 2 minute seg-
ments. Another finding was additionally established that functional connectivity between frontal
and temporal scalp regions provided the most significant differences between MDD and HC classes.
The wavelet feature set obtained the worst classification performance compared to the other EEG
feature sets and the delta and theta powers provided the most significant difference between MDD
and HC samples in all brain regions. They specifically estimate that MDD cases have lower delta
and theta activity compared to HC cases and that MDD cases had lower average alpha powers
than HC in all brain regions. This was additionally indicated by Mumtaz et al. [26]. The authors
additionally found that integrating all the proposed feature sets led to the best classification perfor-
mance, outperforming previous related works. Here they introduced a sequential feature selection
method and found that it positively impacts the classification performance compared to methods
used by related works. Support vector machine with RBF kernel achieved the best performance
compared to other classifiers (LINSVM, LR, DT, NB, RB, GB, RF) and is a common finding by
related works and likewise established by Mahato et al. [32].
This last work, that presents a combination of EEG-derived features, was presented in detail as
it uses a combination of the same mathematical tools for automatic identification of depression
as used by the majority of related studies. Furthermore, it achieves one of the best performances
described at present time using ML models. In literature however, there are numerous of works
that propose other approaches for automatic identification of depression. A brief presentation is
provided as follows, to complete the concise overview of the methodology proposed for automatic
identification of depression.
In 2015, Acharya et al. [34] published a paper considering the efforts of automatic detection of
depression using EEG signals with a focus on non-linear algorithms. The authors propose that
non-linear algorithms such as sample bispectrum magnitude and sample recurrence plots may be
worth investigating, as these methods may feature the more complex dynamical variations of EEG
signals in contrast to linear methods. Preliminary results were described by the authors but no
related articles have been published. Hence, it remains unclear if bispectral analysis or sample
recurrence plots give more information than traditional linear and non-linear analysis algorithms.
Later in 2018, Acharya et al. [35] proposed the first automated depression recognition method based
on deep learning using a convolutional neural network (CNN) model. The CNN model comprised
of convolutional layers responsible for providing important features, pooling layers responsible for
reducing the size of the feature matrix, and fully-connected layers to ultimately classify 15 depressed
and 15 healthy controls based on bipolar EEG signals.
This approach obtained classification
accuracies of 96.0% and 93.5% for the right and left hemispheres, respectively. Despite the lack
of data, the publication was consistent with the majority of recent literature that depression is
associated with increased right cortex activation. Since then, similar methodologies have been
presented proposing deep learning techniques for automatic identification of depression ie.
by
employing a combination model of CNN and long-short term memory (LSTM) techniques [29].
Deep learning is defined as a hierarchy structure and uses a multi-layered structure of mathematical
processing units to progressively extract higher-level features from the input data. Because deep
learning architecture has the capability of extracting features in automated ways, deep learning
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
15
==PAGE:27==
solutions has become more popular within EEG signal processing, and specifically, due to the
limitations of conventional ML techniques ie. with regard to human intervention and the non-
linear relationship often found in EEG signal analysis. However, with regard to the majority of
papers published since Acharya et al. (2018), utilizing deep learning solutions, it is important to
acknowledge the remarkable drawback of the lack of suﬀicient data that may lead to doubting the
accuracies and the extracted features. Therefore, due to the limited amount of data presented
for this work, conventional ML algorithms were the primary focus of this thesis. Furthermore,
there is no standard theory available to help guide researchers in selecting a proper structure of
neural networks and as the functioning of these solutions are diﬀicult to explain, these methods
may reduce the trust in the final network. Black box models is a simple way of referring to deep
learning models that are suﬀiciently complex to straightforwardly interpret them and longstanding
concerns remain about these predictive methods, especially in health care. In contrast however,
these methods may be able to offer better and more effective processing models once enough
training data is provided.
Table 3.1 provides a summary of related machine learning works reporting on automatic EEG-
based diagnosis of depression. In particular, the table includes the study and year of publication,
the sample size used, in terms of subjects with MDD and healthy controls (HC), the EEG mon-
tage applied during data acquisition, number of electrodes and length of the data acquired. The
table also includes ways of data preparation, features extracted, adopted ML models and their
achievements in terms of accuracy, sensitivity and specificity.
3.1.1
Findings of Depression Biomarkers
The state-of-the-art findings of depression biomarkers have been widely studied given the complex,
heterogeneous nature of the disease and are important to consider for both diagnostic purposes and
treatment prediction. In an extensive review conducted by Aguiar Neto et al. [31], a systematic
mapping of more than 250 recent studies (from 2014 to 2018) was made, which use non-invasive
scalp EEG to detect depression biomarkers. Of the large ensemble of studies, results of 42 studies
was selectively discussed based on the authors criteria and included several findings by categoriz-
ing potential biomarkers into six categories: band power, alpha symmetry, signal based features,
network based features, evoked potentials and other biomarkers.
One approach to identify biomarkers using non-invasive EEG is to apply classification algorithms
and predict the state of individuals using a set of features. If the combination of specific features or
a single feature gives a high prediction accuracy, it may be an important biomarker of depression.
It is however diﬀicult to accurately compare the performance of different studies as several incon-
sistencies are present with regard to the setup of experiments, electrode positioning and montage,
the sampling rate, sample sizes, subject characteristics (ages and gender of study participants),
drug use (medicated or not) and diagnosis using a variety of questionnaires. Inconsistencies also
arise as researchers typically have their own agenda ie. focusing on how to automate the diagnosis
of depression, use fewer electrodes or investigate the functional and physiological differences in the
brain.
At the present time, the training of classification algorithms must be performed for every new
dataset and the accuracy of findings in the test phase vary quite a lot. This is primarily due
to a disproportion in the amount of variables used for training compared to the limited number
of study participants and the diﬀiculty of reproducibility. It is important to acknowledge that
authors often miss providing detailed implementation aspects in their publication ie. details on
applied algorithms, the frequency range of interest, which features are used for classification or how
data is split in terms of training and testing classification algorithms, which may have significant
implications on the performance. Without these smaller pieces of information, it is diﬀicult for re-
searchers to re-implement the algorithms, determine their robustness and establish future research
directions. So far, to decide which algorithms to use has been rather pragmatic, meaning that the
authors have had to publish the methods which work. However, despite all of these methodologies,
agreements of some common biomarkers have been defined. An overview of the key findings within
each category of biomarkers have been included below and summarized in Table 3.2.

3.1.1.1
Band power
This approach directly originates from an analysis of the EEG signal in the frequency domain, also
called spectral analysis. The principal EEG frequency bands relating to functional characteristics
and their approximate spectral boundaries have been included in Table 2.1. According to Aguiar
Neto et al. [31], the majority of studies have found that the depressed brain seem to have a more
random network structure which may among other things affect the frequency of neural oscillations.
Specifically, the alpha band seem to have good diagnostic capabilities with increased band power in
depressed compared to healthy controls [28]. Other frequency bands have also been identified but
may be highly dependent on electrode selection and placement. For instance by using 3 electrodes
at the forehead, Cai et al. [2] found that alpha-based features have less prediction power than beta,
theta and gamma based features. Inter-study comparisons, especially with suitable sample sizes,
are therefore necessary to improve future research directions and standardize the methodological
procedures [44].
3.1.1.2
Alpha asymmetry
Alpha asymmetry, which measures the relative alpha band activity between the cerebral hemi-
spheres, has been of frequent interest in studies of depression for years (especially in the frontal
area). However, inconsistent results indicate that this biomarker presents with little to no diag-
nostic capability and is susceptible to other illnesses such as anxiety. It is however likely to predict
specific symptoms of depression such as mood swings and is specifically related to the lack of
activity in the left side of the brain [31].
3.1.1.3
Signal based features
Signal based features may be studied as biomarkers of depression and good discriminators include
Higuchi’s fractal dimension (HFD) as well as detrended fluctuation analysis (DFA) proposed by
Hosseinifard et al. [28] and Bairy et al. [41]. The HFD is one method of calculating the fractal
dimension of a signal, while DFA indicates the long range correlation of the non-stationary EEG
18
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:30==
signals. Both signal based features are non-linear and have provided good accuracies in depression
diagnostics. Other non-linear signal based features include C0-complexity, correlation dimension
(CD), Lempel-Ziv complexity and the largest Lyapunov exponent.
The incomplete understanding of the functionality of the brain have caused a great deal of uncer-
tainty in whether the brain operates in a linear or non-linear fashion, or indeed in a way which
escapes the current modeling methods. However, as the brain have remained incompletely under-
stood for years, this level of understanding indicates that the processes of the brain operates with
enormous complexity. Therefore, non-linear signal based features have typically been considered
superior to linear signal based features.
3.1.1.4
Network based features
With regard to network based features capturing the activity of populations of neurons, depressed
subjects seem to have more random networks. However, like the authors of Aguiar Neto et al.
[31], it is important to emphasize that further studies are needed to evaluate the direct impact
of depression on brain network functionality due to multiple conflicting findings. The network
features described in literature include coherence, which measures linear dependencies between
electrode channels and indicate synchronous oscillations or other methods such as synchronization
likelihood, which reflects how strongly electrode channels are synchronized to all other channels.
3.1.1.5
Event-related potentials (ERPs)
Event-related potentials or evoked potentials is an electrical response in the brain following the
presentation of a stimulus such as a light flash, images or a tone and may reflect differences in
brain functioning between depressed and healthy subjects. They have been found to be particularly
useful when related to more specific symptoms such as memory alterations, emotional processing or
delusional thinking. Related literature on depression typically include the P300 evoked potentials,
which is a positive deflection in the EEG approximately 300 ms after some odd stimulus is presented
[44].
3.1.1.6
Other biomarkers
Other biomarkers of depression include sleep disturbances and stronger pupillary reaction to emo-
tional faces.
However, only a small subset of studies include these as potential biomarkers of
depression and it must be emphasized that other possible physiological reactions may additionally
help understand the mechanisms of depression [31].
3.2
Proposed Approach
The analysis presented in this chapter leads to the conclusion that various algorithms may be
suitable for detection of depression but must be built to maintain performance with different
variations of data and must be reproducible to help establish future research directions. Hence, to
address these considerations, the algorithm proposed in this thesis will consider a combination of
commonly proposed methodologies to compare research methods and identify their generalization
ability.
The approach will be based on signal pre-processing, feature extraction, feature selection, classifi-
cation using a private dataset (also referred to as Dataset A) provided by Dr. Abul Hassan, NED
University of Engineering and Technology, Pakistan, containing resting-state EEG data before
and after the application of treatment with tDCS. A secondary public dataset (also referred to
as Dataset B) provided by Mumtaz et al. [27] will be utilized to evaluate the proposed method-
ology. Proposing new approaches and models based on dataset with an insuﬀicient number of
participants without directly comparing the methodology to other independent dataset has been a
methodological shortfall in previous papers and should be included for evaluating the robustness
of models.
Figure 3.1 illustrates an overview of the proposed approach including data acquisition, signal pre-
processing, feature extraction, feature selection and classification. The details and mathematical

EEG signal acquisition of Dataset A and B is introduced in
Chapter 4, while the methodology of the remaining stages are described in Chapter 5 and 6, re-
spectively. Finally, the results and validation of individual machine learning algorithms based on
the proposed methodology are presented in Chapter 7. The individual linear and non-linear fea-
tures of the proposed methods are presented in Table 6.1 (see Chapter 6), from where a subset of
1-5, 10, 15 and 19 features is selected by feature selection algorithms for binary classification into
depressed (MDD) and healthy control (HC) classes. By adjusting the input at various stages, the
performance of the algorithms will help establish future research directions and generalize current
methods for identification of depression.
The proposed approach will consider the use of traditional methods for identifying clinical de-
pression, including state-of-the-art classifiers (ie. SVM, k-NN, LR and LDA) and feature selection
methods based on temporal and spectral features. Furthermore, supplemental methods for spectral
analysis in identifying depression will likewise be included and involves the wavelet transform.
The main advantage of the wavelet transform compared to the Fourier transform (commonly
applied for spectral analysis) is that it provides eﬀicient access of localized spectral information
about the signals. An early implementation of the wavelet transform was presented by Ahmadlou et
al. [43] for automatic identification of depression and since then, related literature have determined
the effectiveness as well as ineffectiveness of the method [4][27][29]. Nevertheless, due to conflicting
results presented by Movahed et al. [29], it has not been established whether the wavelet transform
is truly advantageous in this regard, if combining the wavelet features of different decomposition
levels benefits analysis and how wavelet hyperparameters affect analysis, such as window function
and decomposition level.
To address these issues and determine how the variability in terms of methodologies affect proposed
state-of-the-art machine learning methods, a comprehensive analysis of hyperparameters will be
included in this thesis. The analysis of hyperparameters will include an investigation of eye states,
segmentation lengths, electrode channels, non-linear methods, linear methods, various frequency
bands and the relevance of inter-hemispheric asymmetry for identification of depression. At last,
the overall behavior of the study participants before and after treatment will be investigated for
an evaluation of the effect of tDCS based on the psychometric test scores provided in Table B.1,
Appendix B.1.

Chapter 4
Preliminary Data Overview
This chapter presents a preliminary overview of Dataset A and B, as well as comments on data
acquisition.
An exploratory investigation of the datasets was also included in this thesis and
is presented in Appendix C. In Appendix C, the exploratory analysis intends to identify simple
separation trends between depressed and healthy controls and how these trends are determined by
electrode locations and features of traditional temporal and spectral analysis.
4.1
Data Acquisition
As previously mentioned, it is critical to build algorithms with high generalizability and diversity
to maintain performance with different variations of data. Therefore, both Dataset A and B were
implemented in this work and will be described accordingly. The pre-treatment data of Dataset
A will be used to perform an EEG-based prediction of depression and is considered the main
contributor of the thesis. The data recorded after treatment with tDCS will be used to evaluate
the proposed methodology and observe the overall behavior of the study participants subsequent
to treatment. Unlike the participants of Dataset A, the participants of Dataset B did not receive
tDCS treatment prior to or subsequent to data acquisition. Therefore, Dataset B is only included
for investigating the robustness of the algorithm in distinguishing depressed from healthy controls,
thus providing a better characterization of depression.
The details on the EEG data of both
datasets are provided below.
4.1.1
Dataset A
The research work of this thesis is primarily based on Dataset A and contains resting-state EEG
data obtained from 23 healthy participants and 23 depressive participants (46 in total). Of the 23
depressive participants, 13 were further classified into an active- and sham group (see Table 4.1).
As presented in the table, only a smaller subset of the participants were included in this work and
will be described in Section 4.1.3.1.
The healthy and active group received tDCS stimulation for five days conducted with a frequency
of one session per day. A stimulation of 1 mA was administered for 30 minutes with anode electrode
placement at electrode channel F3 and cathode placement at electrode channel F4 (see Figure 4.1).
The sham group underwent the same stimulation setup procedures but did not receive any stimulus
during the experiment to investigate the psychological benefit rather than any physiological effect.
The EEG data was acquired using the Mitsar NVX-52 device and a Ref 31 A1A2 EEG montage
with 31 channels according to the 10-10 international electrode system (see Figure 4.1). For every
subject, the electrical signals of the brain were recorded for 2 minutes in a relaxed state with
eyes-closed (EC) followed by a break of 1 minute and an equivalent recording with eyes-open
(EO).
The EEG recordings were scheduled before and after the application of tDCS to compare any
neurological changes induced by tDCS. The assessment of participants for the presence of major

self-reported history of neurological disease were excluded from the study. Participants who were
using analgesics or anti-hypertensive medications were also excluded.

4.1.3
Comments on Data Acquisition
At the present time, no other studies have investigated depression and/or treatment response
based on Dataset A, in contrast to Dataset B by Mumtaz et al. [27][16][26][27][46]. Therefore, an
overview of initial findings and their effects have been included in this section.
4.1.3.1
Overview of Dataset A
From an initial inspection of the electrode channels of Dataset A, it was discovered that the elec-
troencephalographic derivations created to display brain activity of depressed subjects (including
S1, S4, S35, S36) and healthy subjects (including S3, S9 and S10) were not based on the same
A1A2 linked-ear referential EEG montage setup. As a result, these subjects were excluded from
further analysis.
Furthermore, data files were found to be wrongly labelled as other subjects and was subsequently
corrected. It was also discovered that some of the data files for the subjects were in fact missing.
Some of the subjects in the pre-treatment data folders did not exist in the post-treatment data
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
23
==PAGE:35==
folders and likewise, some of the data files for eyes-open and eyes-closed data were also missing.
An overview of excluded, missing and available depressed (active & sham) and healthy control
subjects is presented in Table 4.1.
As a result of incorrect montage setup and missing data files, 10 and 7 study participants were
removed from the pre- and post-treatment datasets, respectively. Furthermore, an additional 11
and 9 participants were removed from the pre- and post-treatment datasets due to high levels of
noise. Hence, the final pre-treatment dataset consisted of 25 subjects in total (HC: 14, MDD: 11),
while the final post-treatment dataset consisted of 30 subjects in total (HC: 15, MDD: 15). For
analysis across the pre- and post-treatment datasets, 20 subject pairs remained (MDD Active: 7,
MDD Sham: 2, HC: 11). Examples of subjects excluded due to noise is presented in Figure B.1,
B.2, B.3, B.4 in Appendix B.2.
Finally, it was additionally discovered that a bandstop (also known as notch) filter of 50 Hz had
been applied to suppress power line noise during data acquisition of some of the subjects, while
the EEG data was digitized with 500 samples per second for all study participants.
4.1.3.2
Overview of Dataset B
The EEG signals of Dataset B provided by Mumtaz et al. [27] was sampled at 256 Hz and bandpass
filtered from 0.1 to 70 Hz with an additional 50 Hz notch filter to suppress power line noise. By
initial inspection of Dataset B, 4 noisy subjects were excluded prior to analysis, including healthy
subject S6 and depressed subjects S1, S20 and S15. These subjects were excluded as they presented
with continuous disrupted brain signals due to various forms and sources of noise similar to the
noisy subjects of Dataset A (see Appendix B.2). Furthermore subjects with missing data in the
eyes-open or eyes-closed state were excluded to enable a fair comparison between the groups. These
subjects included depressed subjects S4, S12, S16, S7 and S8, and healthy subjects S12, S18, S25.
At last, problems existed for importing three healthy subjects, so these were also not considered
and consist of subjects S20, S10 and S17. Thus, the final dataset consisted of 26 depressed subjects
and 23 healthy controls.
