Chapter 5
Wavelet Analysis
In this chapter, the mathematical background of the wavelet transform and an explanation of its
main properties will be introduced, including the window function (also known as mother wavelet)
and decomposition levels. Based on these properties, an algorithm is developed and described in
Chapter 6 for comparison of the wavelet transform to traditional temporal and spectral methods.
In this regard, a brief description of the Fourier transform and Short-time Fourier transform will
be presented to acquaint the reader with the traditional concepts of spectral analysis. Finally, the
foundation of the proposed wavelet algorithm will be presented.
5.1
The Wavelet Transform
The fundamental idea of the wavelet transform is to allow an analysis of signals which contain high
frequency components over short time spans and low frequency components over large time spans.
This makes the wavelet transform ideal for analysis of non-stationary EEG signals and provides
several advantages compared to traditional methods including the Fourier transform (FT) and
short-time Fourier transform (STFT). The FT is widely used to analyze frequency components of
stationary signals and processes where the properties are statistically invariant over time. The FT
of a signal x(t) is presented by
X(ω) = FT[x(t)] =
! +∞
−∞
x(t)e−iωtdt
(5.1)
The FT decomposes a signal into single frequency components and requires all information about
the signal to evaluate its spectral density. While this is an effective method for analyzing the
overall spectral components of signals, it does not provide information about the time localization
of these. This is important for most biomedical applications, as researchers are typically interested
in signal abnormalities or how these signals vary with time. To solve this problem, the short-time
Fourier transform (STFT) was proposed and is defined by
X(τ, ω) = STFT[x(t)] =
! +∞
−∞
x(t)w(t −τ)e−iωtdt
(5.2)
The STFT is a natural extension of the FT and includes a window function w(τ) that selects
a smaller part of the signal x(t) using the translation parameter τ. The signal x(t) is divided
into smaller segments of equal length and by multiplying these with the window w(τ), the FT is
computed for every segment for particular frequencies f, as ω = 2πf and is the angular frequency.
A concern of the STFT is that resolution is fixed according to the width of the window function
(see Figure 5.1) . A wide window gives a good frequency resolution but poor time resolution, while
a narrower window gives a better time resolution but poor frequency resolution. To overcome
these issues, the wavelet transform was proposed to allow narrow windows for higher frequencies
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
25
==PAGE:37==
(as these change more rapidly), and wide windows for lower frequencies (to recognize the slower
variations in the signal). The wavelet transform is presented by
X(τ, a) = Wa[x(t)] =
1
"
|a|
! +∞
−∞
x(t)ψ∗(t −τ
a
) dt,
(5.3)
where the transformed signal X(τ, a) is a function of the translation parameter τ and the scale
parameter a. The mother wavelet is denoted by ψ, where the ∗indicates that the complex conjugate
is used in case of a complex wavelet. The signal energy is normalized at every scale by the factor
1/√a and ensures that the wavelets have the same energy [47]. The mother wavelet is modified
by the translation parameter τ and dilated by changing the scale parameter a. As a increases,
the width of the mother wavelet increases and this affects the fixed resolution. As the width of
the mother wavelet increases by increasing a, the time resolution decreases while the frequency
resolution increases and vice versa for smaller values of a. The translation parameter τ specifies
the location of the wavelet in time and by changing τ the wavelet is shifted along the signal. The
elements in X(τ, a) are called wavelet coeﬀicients and are associated to a frequency (scale) and a
point in the time domain. As for the FT and STFT, the wavelet transform also has an inverse
transformation (ICWT) given by
x(t) =
1
C2

−∞
X(τ, a) 1

) dτda,
(5.4)
where Cψ is known as the admissibility constant and is a mathematical criteria a wavelet function
ψ(t) must meet [47]. The details of wavelet criteria will not be covered in this thesis.
As presented in (5.4), the inverse wavelet transform may be used to reconstruct signals from the
wavelet coeﬀicients. For analysis purposes however, a discretization is usually done by applying the
discrete wavelet transform using a filter bank and special filters for the analysis and reconstruction
of signals.

wavelet analysis. Processing signals in the time- or in the frequency domain does not provide infor-
mation of how the spectral properties of signals change with time. The STFT yields a uniform tiling
of the time-frequency domain but is limited to a fixed window while the wavelet analysis achieves
a multi-resolution decomposition by enhancing the time localization of the signal characteristics
for higher frequencies at the expense of frequency localization and vice versa.
5.1.1
Discrete Wavelet Transform
The discrete wavelet transform usually involves a dyadic grid and provides a simple and eﬀicient
method for discretization [47]. For discrete time signals x(n), the dyadic discrete wavelet transform
is equivalent to an octave filter bank and may be implemented according to low-pass and high-pass
finite impulse response (FIR) filters [48].
26
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:38==
The low- and high-pass filters split the frequency content of the input signal in half, leaving two
output signals with the equivalent amount of samples. As the two outputs together contain the
same frequency content as the input signal and the amount of samples has doubled, a down-
sampling of the signals is performed by a factor 2 (denoted by ↓2).
The procedure is then
repeated by sending the low-pass filtered signal to the next stage depending on the expected level
of decomposition [47]. An example of 3-level decomposition is presented in Figure 5.2.

The output signals are called wavelet coeﬀicient subbands (denoted by c) and retrieve the ap-
proximations A and details D of the input signal. The approximation subband cA contains the
lowest frequencies of the signal while the detail subbands cD provide the detailed information of
the signal. The frequency content of the approximation frequency band cA and detail frequency
band cD may be calculated as
fcA = [0, 2−p−1fs],
(5.5)
fcD = [2−p−1fs, 2−pfs],
(5.6)
where p is the level of decomposition and fs is the sampling frequency [47]. An example of the
frequency content at each level based on a signal sampled with 256 samples per second is visualized
in Figure 5.3 for 5-level decomposition into approximate- (cA5) and detail (cD1−cD5) coeﬀicients.

5.1.2
Choice of the Mother Wavelet
In literature, several mother wavelets have been proposed including the Morlet wavelet, the
Daubechies wavelet, the Coiflet wavelet and several others. The wavelets are brief wave-like oscil-
lations with amplitudes starting at zero and then they increase or decrease until they return back
to zero once or multiple times. Depending on the number and direction of these oscillations, they
posses specific properties which make them useful for processing signals such as the EEG signals
of the brain.
In depression analysis and within related research areas, there are currently no rules for determining
which mother wavelet is appropriate for specific applications, and based on previous work for
detection of depression by Mumtaz et al. [27], Puthankattil et al. [49] and Faust et al. [4] a variety
of wavelets have been investigated. Nevertheless, one intuitive rule have been established as a rule
of thumb for choosing an appropriate wavelet:
• The mother wavelet should resemble the shape of the signal of interest
As presented by the rule of thumb, the concept behind most applications of wavelet analysis has
been selecting the mother wavelet most similar to the signal of interest. Larger values are obtained
for the wavelet transform when the wavelet coincides with a segment of similar shape as the mother
wavelet. In this regard, a mother wavelet resembling the signal of interest is used to retain the
original signal and enhance the frequency spectrum of the signal by removal of noise components
[50].
Mumtaz et al. [27] chose the Daubechies 4 (Db4) mother wavelet based on the findings of related
work and as this particular wavelet provided with the highest classification accuracy compared
to other wavelet functions.
Puthankattil et al.
[49] performed a correlation analysis between
23 wavelets and EEG signals and found that the Coiflet 5 (Coif5) wavelet yielded the highest
correlation coeﬀicient. Therefore, based on this similarity measure, this wavelet was selected for
analysis and this choice was also supported by Movahed et al. [29]. The final example of wavelets
for depression analysis is Faust et al. [4] that used the Daubechies 8 (Db8) wavelet without any
particular justification. However, as described in this thesis in the chapter of related work, Faust
et al. [4] obtained an accuracy of 99.5%. Therefore, to help guide future research directions for
depression analysis, the Db4, Db8 and the Coif5 wavelets are investigated in this work.
5.1.3
Proposed Wavelet Algorithm
To evaluate the EEG signals of Dataset A and B presented in this thesis, a 5-level wavelet de-
composition according to the presented filter bank was implemented to obtain wavelet coeﬀicient
subbands (cD1 −cD5 and cA5) for separate scales according to the frequency bands of the brain
(see Figure 5.4).
For optimal wavelet decomposition into these frequency bands, the EEG signals of Dataset A
were down-sampled from 500 to 256 Hz prior to wavelet analysis.
This was done using linear
interpolation and is described in the following chapter (see Section 6.1.2). Based on the down-
sampled signals, the final frequency bands for wavelet analysis were established (see Table 5.1),
including the corresponding decomposed signals and EEG frequency bands.
As proposed by related work such as Puthankattil et al. [49], the decomposed signals (D1 −D5
and A5) were computed by reconstructing individual wavelet coeﬀicient subbands. This was done
by applying a synthesis filter bank [47].
The filter bank was based on the octave filter bank,
as previously described, but instead of down-samling, the wavelet coeﬀicient subbands were up-
sampled (↑2) and then passed through filters L′(z) and H′(z), similar to L(z) and H(z). To
prevent aliasing and distortion of the signals for perfect reconstruction, a number of filter design
criteria was met [47]. However, these will not be covered in this thesis.
The first reconstructed signal D1 is considered to mainly include the noise components of the
EEG signal, while the remaining signals D2–D5 and A5 is considered to include signal information
related to each EEG frequency band (see Table 5.1). An example of the final decomposed signals
based on the Db8 mother wavelet is presented in Figure 5.5. By summation of the individual
28
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:40==
decomposed wavelet signals (D1 + D2 + D3 + D4 + D5 + A5), the original input signal may be
easily reconstructed.
In Figure 5.6, the decomposed signal corresponding to A5 is visualized in relation to the input
signal. Here, it is clear that A5 contains the lower frequencies of the input signal.
The wavelet filter banks were implemented using the PyWavelets library [51] and built-in wavelet
families including Daubechies (Db4 and Db8) and Coiflets (Coif5) as described above in Section
5.1.2. To successfully apply the discrete wavelet transform, some extrapolation was performed to
extend the signal borders. The implementation was based on symmetric padding, where the signal
is extended by mirroring samples. The extrapolation method must be carefully considered to avoid
significant artifacts at the borders of the signals [51].

eyes open, pre-treatment, electrode channel C3-A1, window size from 12-22 seconds.

Chapter 6
Algorithm Description
In the previous chapter, the mathematical background of the wavelet transform and the influence
of the decomposition level and mother wavelet was provided to understand the basic principles
behind its implementation on EEG signals and for detection of depression. Furthermore, a brief
description of the FT and STFT was presented to acquaint the reader with an overview and key
concepts of spectral analysis. In contrast, temporal analysis refers to analyzing signals in the time
domain and does not require such an introduction.
In this chapter, the methodology of the algorithms developed throughout this project will be
presented. The details and mathematical background of every step of the algorithms will be pre-
sented in relation to the order applied. As presented in Figure 3.1, the proposed framework consists
of data acquisition, signal pre-processing, feature extraction, feature selection and classification.
While data acquisition was thoroughly described in Section 4.1, the remainder will be described
in this chapter. The purpose of this work is to propose an algorithm for off-line analysis and the
computational load of the algorithm is therefore not a primary concern.
6.1
Signal Pre-processing
Signal pre-processing is an important step of the algorithm and seeks to transform the EEG data
into a format that may be more easily interpreted and suitable for analysis. For this purpose,
signal processing techniques including filtering, down-sampling, independent component analysis
(ICA) and segmentation into epochs were implemented accordingly.
6.1.1
Artifact Removal using Filters
The initial step of EEG pre-processing includes artifact removal using bandpass and bandstop
filters. These are used to remove noise, drifts and artifacts represented by unique frequencies and
which mask the signal or interfere with its analysis. While filtering is a common practise in pre-
processing EEG signals, the choice of filter design is not typically specified in related literature
despite the fact that there are no golden standard for their use and that these filters may introduce
temporal distortions in the signals. As a result, the choice of filtering was based on Chebyshev
type II filters, also known as inverse Chebyshev filters.
Filters may be designed to have a finite impulse response (FIR) or infinite impulse response (IIR).
The impulse response characterizes the behavior of the filter and determines how the filter handles
an impulse signal in the time domain. Its Fourier transform is known as the frequency response.
The IIR filters (including Chebyshev type II filters) are typically more computationally eﬀicient
compared to the FIR filters but may introduce unequal delays at different frequencies due to
nonlinear phase characteristics. The FIR filters on the other hand produce equal delays at all
frequencies, known as a linear phase response. A description of the particular filter designs and
frequency characteristics implemented in this work is presented in the following sections.
Figure 6.1 displays an example of before and after filtering the EEG signals with bandstop (BS) and
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
31
==PAGE:43==
bandpass (BP) filters. Likewise, the result of filtering the signals displayed using power spectral
density (PSD) is presented in Figure 6.2.
Figure 6.1: An example of filtering EEG signals with bandstop (BS) and bandpass (BP) filters.
The top figure presents the original signal (blue) and the BS filtered signal (orange). The bottom
figure presents the resulting BS and BP filtered EEG signal (black). The BS filter was used to
suppress powerline interference at 50 Hz and the BP filter was applied to remove low- (below
0.5 Hz) and high-frequency noise (above 70 Hz). Data used: S4 (depressed) of Dataset A with
eyes-closed prior to treatment with tDCS.
Figure 6.2: Power spectral density plot of 29 electrode channels. The filter order is 12 (originally
6) for both filters using Chebyshev type II digital filter designs and cascaded second-order sections.

remove low- and high-frequency noise ((below 0.5 Hz and above 70 Hz, respectively). Data used:

6.1.1.1
Bandstop filter
The bandstop filter (Chebyshev type II) was applied to suppress powerline interference at 50 Hz.
Powerline interference is characterized by a sinusoidal component at 50/60 Hz and is usually the
result of electrical devices present during data acquisition. The filter characteristics and frequency
response of the filter is visualized in Appendix D.1, Figure D.1a.
6.1.1.2
Bandpass filter
A bandpass filter (Chebyshev type II) was additionally applied to minimize non‐neural low‐frequency
drift and high-frequency noise. Low-frequency drift, also known as baseline drift, is characterized
by a change in the baseline position of the signal and may be removed by suppressing the non-neural
frequencies below 0.5 Hz. High-frequency noise was likewise suppressed by removing frequencies
above 70 Hz, which is an edge frequency commonly used for detection of depression and other
studies of the brain. The particular filter characteristics and frequency response of the filter is
visualized in Appendix D.1, Figure D.1b.
To to ensure stability of both IIR filters, second-order-sections (sos) were implemented as well as
forward-backward filters that applies the filters once forward and once backwards. The combined
forward-backward filter has a filter order twice that of the original but zero phase to ensure no
phase distortion and constant group delay.
6.1.2
Down-sampling
For Dataset A, down-sampling was conducted from 500 to 256 Hz to prepare the EEG signals for
optimal wavelet decomposition into frequency bands similar to the frequency bands of the brain.
Furthermore, as frequencies above 70 Hz was suppressed using the bandpass filter, frequency
components higher than 140 Hz were not required for analysis to avoid aliasing (according to the
Nyquist Theorem). Down-sampling was conducted using linear interpolation and a comparison
between the original filtered signal and the down-sampled signal is visualized in Figure 6.3. In the
figure, it is seen that down-sampling from 500 to 256 Hz does not affect the quality of the original
signal as it hides perfectly underneath the down-sampled signal.
Figure 6.3: The results of down-sampling the filtered EEG signals from 500 Hz to 256 Hz using
linear interpolation. Data used: S4 (depressed) of Dataset A with eyes-closed prior to treatment
with tDCS.
6.1.3
Artifact Removal using Independent Component Analysis
After visual inspection of the EEG signals, it was found that these signals include other artifacts
such as pulse artifacts, EMG artifacts, movement artifacts, eye blink- and eye movement artifacts.
These artifacts are not represented by unique frequencies in terms of bandpass and bandstop
filtering and must be removed by other means. Therefore, as performed by Movahed et al. [29],
Wu et al. [36] and Mahato et al. [32], the artifacts were removed using independent component
analysis (ICA).
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
33
==PAGE:45==
ICA is a computational technique which attempts to decompose a mixture of signals into their
independent sources. It is typically explained by the cocktail party problem or blind source sepa-
ration problem, where underlying speech signals are separated from recordings consisting of people
talking simultaneously at a cocktail party. This is typically applied in EEG analysis for its ability
to filter out underlying artifacts from the EEG signals.
ICA is based on three assumptions:
1. The source signals are statistically independent of each other
2. The source signals have non-Gaussian distributions
3. The mixing system is determined (the number of inputs equals the number of outputs)
The FastICA algorithm presented by the scikit-learn library [52] was used for this work as an
eﬀicient algorithm for ICA. The principle target of the algorithm is to find sources which have
non-Gaussian distributions. Therefore, the algorithm also includes centering and whitening the
data as pre-processing steps to bring the mean to zero (centering) and remove any correlations in
the data (whitening). With regard to hyperparameters of the algorithm, the tolerance level was
0.001 and the maximum number of iterations during fit was 2000 to allow the algorithm to fully
converge. In other respects, the algorithm was implemented using default settings.
The result of performing ICA on the EEG signals of S14 is visualized in Figure 6.4. The signals have
been marked accordingly, indicating the locations where eye blinks have successfully been removed
from the signals. These are particularly visible in the prefrontal electrode channels Fpz-A1, Fp1-A1
and Fp2-A2 located right above the forehead. Pulse artifacts were likewise removed from the signals
by inspecting the independent components in the time as well as frequency domain (see Figure
D.2a and D.2b in Appendix D.2).
Figure 6.4: Artifact removal using ICA. The figure presents examples on locations where eye blinks
on the Pre ICA signals have successfully been removed from the EEG signals Post ICA. Data used:
Participant S14 (healthy) of Dataset A with eyes-open prior to treatment with tDCS.
6.1.4
Segmentation
Segmentation of the data into smaller epochs was performed to provide machine learning models
with additional new and diverse instances for training. Segmentation was additionally a way to
34
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:46==
reduce overfitting of the models and increase their generalization ability. To evaluate the conse-
quence of segmentation in depression analysis, epoch lengths of 2 s, 10 s, 60 s and 120 s were
evaluated based on similar lengths used by related literature [32][29][27][2].
6.2
Feature Extraction
Feature extraction was performed to reduce the dimensionality of the data and to eﬀiciently rep-
resent the data in such a way that appropriate information about the EEG signals are captured
for depression analysis. The features implemented in the feature extraction process for identifica-
tion of depression according to temporal, wavelet and Fourier analysis are presented in Table 6.1
and include linear and non-linear state-of-the-art features. Some of these features were added to
smaller and fixed feature set presented in Table 6.2. To decide which features to include in the
fixed feature set was partly unsystematic and selected in accordance with features proposed by
related work.
The features were extracted from 3, 4, 6 and 19 electrode channels per subject, depending on the
analysis at hand (ie. investigating the optimal selection of electrode channels, classifiers etc.). In
this regard, equivalent electrode channels were selected across the datasets to enable a comparison
of the results.
The number and location of the electrode channels were selected according to
electrode channels proposed by related literature (see Table 6.3).
Some electrode channels of
Dataset A did not exist in Dataset B and vice versa. In this case, similar channels were selected
with close proximity to the area of interest and depending on the channels proposed by related
work. In this work, the electrode channels with closest proximity were assumed to present with
equal diagnostic value for detection of depression.

6.2.1
Linear Features
The linear features of this work include simple and statistical features, absolute power and inter-
hemispheric asymmetry. Simple and statistical features are used to summarize the signal char-
acteristics, absolute power features are used to estimate their main frequency components and
inter-hemispheric asymmetry features are used to evaluate the asymmetrical activity between the
left and right hemisphere of the brain. The linear features were extracted for temporal and spectral
analysis based on the Fourier and wavelet transform.
6.2.1.1
Simple and Statistical Features
To summarize the characteristics of the signals in terms of spread and tendency, statistical features
including the mean, standard deviation, variance, kurtosis, skewness and Hjorth parameters were
computed for every epoch. Other fundamental features including the maximum peak, number of
zero-crossings and energy were also computed to evaluate the differences between depressed and
healthy controls as proposed by Movahed et al. [29] and Cai et al. [2].
The mean is a measure of the central tendency of the signal, while the variance and standard
deviation are interchangeable measures of variability. In this regard, the variance is a measure
of dispersion, ie., how much the observations vary from the mean, and the standard deviation is
the square root of variance. Kurtosis and skewness were used to describe the distribution of the
observations in the signals. Kurtosis is a measure of the shape of a distribution’s tails in relation
to its overall shape, whereas skewness refers to a distortion or lack of symmetry of the observations
36
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:48==
in relation to the normal distribution.
The Hjorth parameters consist of three main measures called activity (h0), mobility (h1) and
complexity (h2) and are defined as follows:
h0 = var(x(n)),
(6.1)
h1 =
#
h0(x′(n))
h0(x(n)) ,
(6.2)
h2 = h1(x′(n))
h1(x(n)) ,
(6.3)
where x(n) is the time-series signal, var is the variance and x′(n) is the first order derivative of
x(n). The activity measure represents the signal power and is the variance of the time-series, the
mobility measure represents the mean frequency or the proportion of standard deviation of the
power spectrum, while the complexity measure represents the change in frequency.
At last, the energy, maximum peak amplitude and number of zero-crossings of the signals were
computed. For discrete-time signals, the energy (Ex) is given by
Ex =
N−1
$
n=0
|x(n)|2,
(6.4)
where x(n) is the time-series signal and N is the total number of samples. For transparency, most
simple and statistical measures were computed using established libraries, including SciPy, NumPy
and AntroPy [53] and will not be covered in this thesis.
6.2.1.2
Absolute Power
The absolute power fluctuations in the signals were evaluated at different brain regions and con-
ducted based on Welch’s method for estimating power spectra. Furthermore, the absolute power
was evaluated within five EEG frequency bands as presented in in Table 6.4. As seen in the table,
the band limits have been altered slightly from the traditional band limits presented in Table 2.1
to match the decomposition levels of the wavelet transform presented in the previous chapter. This
was done to promote a more fair comparison between the two methodologies.
Table 6.4: Frequency bands of EEG investigated in this work including the delta, theta, alpha,
beta, gamma and all frequency bands.
Delta band
Theta band
Alpha band
Beta band
Gamma band
All bands
0.5 - 4 Hz
4 - 8 Hz
8 - 16 Hz
16 - 32 Hz
32 - 70 Hz
0.5 - 70 Hz
Welch’s method for estimating power spectra was applied using the Scipy library in Python and
implemented based on the Hanning window function. Depending on the length of the signal epochs,
ie. 2, 10, 60 and 120 seconds, the signal was split into segments of 2 seconds with 50% overlap
to estimate the power within each frequency band. More specifically, Welch’s method works by
splitting the signal into smaller overlapping segments and applying a window function (in the time
domain). Most window functions, including the Hanning window, give more influence to the values
at the center of the function than to the values at the edges of the function. This represents a loss
of information and to mitigate this loss, the segments are typically overlapped with each other.
After doing so, a spectrum of the segments are computed using the discrete FT and by squaring
the magnitude of the result. The individual spectrum are then averaged, resulting in an array
of power measurements vs. frequency (in bins) that may be evaluated for analysis. As a result,
Welch’s method reduces the variance and non-systematic noise of the power measurements.
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
37
==PAGE:49==
6.2.1.3
Inter-hemispheric Asymmetry
Inter-hemispheric asymmetry of the whole brain and paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry of specific
brain regions were computed for all six frequency bands presented in Table 6.4. The asymmetry of
the brain hemispheres may be represented by the difference in band power of left hemisphere and
right hemisphere and specifically, in accordance with Mahato et al. [32] and Al-Kaysi et al. [5],
it may be obtained by subtracting the natural logarithm of the power of the left hemisphere with
the same of the right hemisphere. As a result, inter-hemispheric asymmetry (Ahemis) is given by
Ahemis = Mean(log(Pright) −log(Pleft)),
(6.5)
where P is the average band power of the left and right electrode channels, respectively. Likewise,
paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry was computed for each brain region including the prefrontal,
frontal, central, temporal, parietal and occipital region. The particular electrode channels used
for both measures are specified in Table 6.5. As a result, paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry
(Apaired) is presented by
Apaired = log(Pright) −log(Pleft),
(6.6)
where P is the average band power of right and left electrode channels, respectively. For both
equations, positive values represent increased activity of the right hemisphere, while negative values
represent increased activity of the left hemisphere. For transparency, the average power of the
electrode channels used for the asymmetry measures was based on standard FFT-based estimations
and whole signal epochs, ie. 2, 10, 60 and 120 seconds, in contrast to using Welch’s method. This
was due to initial investigations entirely based on stationary epochs of 2 seconds and was not
changed for subsequent analysis.

left) investigated for

6.2.2
Non-linear Features
The non-linear features of this work include state-of-the-art entropy measures, detrended fluctu-
ation analysis (DFA), Higuchi’s and Katz’ fractal dimension, and Hurst exponent to explore the
complexity and dynamics of brain behavior. The non-linear features were extracted for temporal
and spectral analysis based on the time-series signals and reconstructed wavelet signals, respec-
tively.
6.2.2.1
Entropy Measures
In a general sense, entropy is a concept applied to estimate the amount of uncertainty or random-
ness of patterns and may be applied to time-series like EEG signals to equivalently estimate the
predictability of signals and the amount of information contained in them. However, a number of
methods and parameter choices exist for computing the entropy of signals and may fundamentally
change the output and meaning of the concept. Based on related work, four entropy measures
were included in this thesis to evaluate the importance of these features for detection of depres-
sion. Specifically, Faust et al. [4] obtained one of the highest classification performances of 99.5%
based on entropy measures and wavelet analysis.
Spectral entropy is a concept applied to estimate the amount of uncertainty of the signal in the
frequency domain. Spectral entropy requires the PSD of the signal and is given by
38
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:50==
H(x, fs) = −
fs/2
$
f=0
P(f)log2(P(f)),
(6.7)
where P is the normalized power spectral density (PSD) of a signal and fs is the sampling frequency
[53]. For a periodic signal including one frequency, the spectral entropy will be close to zero, while
for white noise, the measure will be high, as white noise contains power in all frequencies.
Unlike spectral entropy, approximate and sample entropy is commonly used for analyzing entropy
in the time domain. Sample entropy is a modification of approximate entropy and differs from
approximate entropy by not involving self-matching and pairwise matching of segment similarity.
However, as both measures are commonly applied for depression analysis using EEG signals, both
measures were included in the thesis.
The sample entropy of a signal is defined by
H(x, m, r) = −logC(m + 1, r)
C(m, r)
,
(6.8)
where m is the embedding dimension, r is the threshold for similarity (ie. radius of the neigh-
borhood), C(m + 1, r) is the number of embedded vectors of length m + 1 having a Chebyshev
distance inferior to r and C(m, r) is the number of embedded vectors of length m + 1 having a
Chebyshev distance inferior to r [53]. In this research, the values of m and r were chosen to 2 and
0.2 std(x), respectively.
Similarly, permutation entropy is a complexity measure used for analyzing entropy in the time-
domain and has been proposed by related literature for depression analysis. Permutation entropy
captures the order of values in a signal and extracts a probability distribution of these patterns for
subsequent entropy computation. While smaller values of these measures indicate the repetitiveness
of the signal, higher values indicate irregularity or randomness. The permutation entropy of a signal
is defined by
H = −
$
p(π)log2(π)
(6.9)
where the sum runs over n! permutations π with order n [53]. For a comprehensive description of
these entropy measures however, please refer to the AntroPy library adapted for these computations
[53].
6.2.2.2
Detrended Fluctuation Analysis (DFA)
DFA is a mathematical approach to estimate the long-term statistical dependencies in a time-series
signal. It is related to techniques such as autocorrelation and the Fourier transform and may be
applied to non-stationary signals whose dynamics change with time such as EEG signals.
A time-series signal x(i); i = 1, 2, ..., N is integrated prior to analysis
y(k) =
k
$
i=1
x(i) −¯x
(6.10)
where y(k) is the integrated signal over an interval k = 1, 2, 3, ..., N and ¯x is the mean of the
time-series. The integrated signal y(k) is then divided into n equal segments and detrended by
subtracting the confined trend yn(k) from y(k) in each segment. The fluctuation of the detrended
time series y(k) is then calculated given by
F(n) =
%
&
&

N
N
$
k=1
(y(k) −yn(k))2

39

This process is repeated over a range of n segments and a log-log graph of F(n) against n is
constructed. The slope of a straight line fit to the graph (known as α) is then used to extract the
important properties of the signal [41].
DFA was successfully implemented for depression analysis by recent work including Movahed et
al. [29], Mahato et al. [32] and Bachmann et al. [37] and therefore, its usefulness in distinguishing
between depressed and healthy controls is evaluated in this thesis and implemented using to the
AntroPy library [53].
6.2.2.3
Fractal Analysis
Fractal dimension (FD) is a common measure for characterizing EEG signals and is typically
applied for non-linearity and complexity analysis of signals. There are many available algorithms
to calculate FD but Higuchi’s fractal dimension (HFD) and Katz’ fractal dimension (KFD) have
specifically been shown to quantify the structural changes of EEG signals with success. For example
by obtaining classification performances above 90% based on 12 MDD and 12 HC in a study by
Ahmadlou et al. [43]. Another study by Wu et al. [36], based on 200 MDD and 200 HC, indicates
that these measures may be sensitive to noise and found that HFD and KFD did not provide
satisfactory classification performances (max. 62.86%). Nevertheless, a proper interpretation of
the usefulness of these methods have yet to be addressed. Therefore, to explore the usefulness of
these features on Dataset A and B, the algorithms for HFD and KFD were implemented according
to the AntroPy library [53]. As an example of FD, KFD is given by
D = log10(L/a)
log10(d/a
(6.12)
where L is the sum and a is the average of the Euclidean distances between successive points of
the signal. d is the maximum distance between the first sample and any other sample of the signal
[53].
6.2.2.4
Hurst Exponent
The Hurst exponent is a statistical measure used to characterize non-linearity by estimating the
long range dependencies of a time-series signal. The mathematical representation of the Hurst
exponent (H) is given by
H = log(R/S)
log(T)
(6.13)
where T is the sample length of a signal, R is the difference between the maximum and minimum
deviation from the mean, S is the standard deviation and the R/S ratio gives the re-scaled range
[41].
In relation to depression analysis, Acharya et al. [40] found that the Hurst exponent is significantly
higher in depressed individuals compared to healthy controls.
Independently, that same year,
Bairy et al. [41] found that the Hurst exponent was significantly smaller for depressed individuals
compared to the healthy controls (based on decomposed signals of the discrete cosine transform as
opposed to time-series signals). To help establish future research directions, the Hurst exponent
was therefore implemented in this thesis and based on the Nolds library [54].
6.3
Feature Selection
In machine learning applications, the features extracted from EEG signals typically involve redun-
dant and/or irrelevant features that may be highly correlated or does not affect the classification
performance. The large number of features may also increase the computational load significantly
and lead to overfitting issues where the machine learning model is unable to generalize to unseen
examples.
Therefore, feature selection is commonly adapted prior to classification in order to
40
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:52==
select a subset of relevant features, reduce the dimension of the feature space, and improve the
classification performance of the machine learning model.
Depending on the feature selection and classification method employed, normalization may be
performed in advance to transform the the features into a similar scale. This is important as features
with larger scales may dominate others. In this work, normalization was performed according to
min-max normalization presented as
xnorm =
x −xmin
xmax −xmin
,
(6.14)
where x is the feature to be normalized according to its minimum and maximum value.
The
features are transformed to decimals between 0 and 1 without distorting the differences in the
ranges of the values. This may ultimately improve the performance and stability of the model
depending on its underlying framework.
In this section, three feature selection methods are presented including the analysis of variance
(ANOVA) F-test, the sequential forward selection (SFS) algorithm and the minimum redundancy
- maximum relevance (mRMR) algorithm. These algorithms were implemented based on all of
the data of investigation depending on the specific analysis and number of electrode channels etc.
The final feature subset was determined to consist of 1-5, 10, 15 and 19 features for an iterative
classification process to achieve the most accurate model with the least amount of features.
6.3.1
ANOVA F-test
Statistical tests are commonly used to reduce the dimension of the feature space in depression
analysis such as the ANOVA (analysis of variance) or t-test, implemented by Movahed et al.[29],
Acharya et al.[40], Faust et al. [4] and Ahmadlou et al.[43]. The t-test is a type of hypothesis
test that compares the population means of two groups, where the ANOVA is typically used to
compare the means of two or more populations (usually three or more). In this thesis, the ANOVA
method was implemented as a type of F-statistic and may be referred to as an ANOVA f-test. The
scikit-learn library provides an implementation of the ANOVA F-test and was used for feature
selection by selecting the top k most relevant features (where k corresponds to 1-5, 10, 15 and 19
features) [52].
The F-statistic is based on the ratio of mean squares, where ”mean squares” is an estimate of pop-
ulation variance that accounts for the degrees of freedom (DF) used to calculate the population
variance. In essence, it is the variation between population means divided by the variation within
population means. The greater the variation between population means relative to the variation
within the population, the larger the F-statistic. Hence, larger F-statistics suggest a greater dif-
ference between population means and may be used to determine if the features are significantly
different for the compared classes. Like the t-test, ANOVA assumes that the data is normally
distributed.
6.3.2
SFS Algorithm
Forward feature selection is a sequential algorithm that has not been employed in previous work
for depression analysis. In 2021 however, Movahed et al. [29] reported the success of sequential
backward feature selection outperforming previous work in relation to MDD diagnosis based on
time-series EEG signals. For this purpose, sequential forward selection was evaluated and imple-
mented in this work.
Sequential forward selection is principally the opposite of backward feature selection and the steps
of SFS are summarized in Algorithm 1. The algorithm sequentially adds features to form a feature
subset and chooses the best feature that maximizes a criterion function (J) until the feature subset
contains the number of requested features (p). In this work, the criterion function was based on
the 10-fold cross-validation score of classifiers, ie. support vector machine (SVM).
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
41
==PAGE:53==
When the number of requested features is small compared to the total number of features, forward
selection is significantly faster than backward selection. However, a drawback of forward selection
is that it is unable to capture significant interactions between individually useless features.
Algorithm 1: The SFS algorithm.
Input:
Y = {y1, y2, ..., yd}
Output: Xk = {xj|j = 1, 2, ..., k; xj ∈Y }, where k = (0, 1, 2, ..., d)
1. Start with an empty set, X0 = ∅where k = 0
2. Add a feature x+ = arg max J(Xk + x), where x ∈Y −Xk
3. Update Xk+1 = Xk + x+; k = k + 1
4. Continue until k = p, where p is the number of requested features
6.3.3
mRMR Algorithm
The mRMR algorithm (also known as the minimum redundancy - maximum relevance algorithm)
is a simple and iterative algorithm which attempts to find a small subset of features that are
minimally redundant with each other but maximally relevant with respect to the target variable.
The mRMR algorithm was presented related work such as Cai et al. [2] and Mumtaz et al. [27] and
must be evaluated to establish its usefulness compared to other methods in depression analysis.
Under the mRMR framework, four variants are typically discussed depending on its usage for
discrete or continuous features. The F-test correlation quotient (FCQ) variant will be explained
in relation to the continuous features considered in this work. The FCQ is based on the F-statistic
to estimate the relevance, and correlation to estimate the redundancy. By combining relevance
with redundancy the method considers temporal information in the feature selection process which
leads to a selection of more discriminative features.
Consider m features for a given feature Xi (i ∈{1, 2, ..., m}). Then feature importance based on
the mRMR criterion is given by
f mRMR(Xi) =
F(Y, Xi)
1
|S|
(
Xs∈S
ρ(Xs, Xi)
,
(6.15)
where Y is the target class, S is the selected features, |S| is the number of features, Xs ∈S is one
feature out of the feature set S and Xi denotes a feature currently not selected Xi /∈S. ρ(Xs, Xi)
denotes the Pearson correlation and and F(Y, Xi) is the F-statistic [55]. In the iterative process
of selecting the features with the highest importance score maxf mRMR(Xi), the features will be
added to the selected feature set S. In this work, the algorithm was implemented according to the
pymRMRe package [56].
6.4
Classification
Classification is a supervised machine learning concept which predicts and categorizes data into
classes. For example, identification of depression may be identified as a classification problem with
only two classes (depressed and healthy control). The binary classification algorithm then utilizes
training data involving both classes to understand how specific input variables relate to each class.
In this thesis, binary classification was performed using top ranked 1-5, 10, 15, and 19 features in
an iterative manner according to an approach proposed by Mumtaz et al. [26]. The optimal feature
subset was based on the highest classification performance involving a 100-time permutation test
with 10-fold cross-validation to improve the robustness of the underlying model. Equivalent per-
mutation tests were likewise applied by Mumtaz et al. [27] [26] [16]. As the 100 iterations resulted
in a 100 different performance evaluation metrics, the final confusion matrix was computed by
averaging all iterations. The optimal feature subset was determined based on the highest accuracy
and was selected as the minimum number of features required for that model. Classification was
42
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)

==PAGE:54==
conducted based on feature matrices including individual features and combinations of features
extracted from temporal, and spectral analysis according to the methodology of related literature.
To help compare the methodology and findings of related literature, combinations of these methods
were explored in conjunction with the most recognized hyperparameters. These hyperparameters
were adjusted throughout analysis according the findings of the algorithms (ie. according to the
best performing classifier, feature selection method etc.).
The final classification models investigated in this work, include support vector machine (SVM),
logistic regression (LR), linear discriminant analysis (LDA) and k-nearest neighbors (k-NN), each
of which will be presented in this section along with a description of the performance evaluation
metrics and k-fold cross-validation.
6.4.1
k-Fold Cross-Validation
k-fold Cross-validation is a common procedure used to evaluate machine learning models on limited
amount of observations. The procedure is influenced by a parameter k, which refers to a number
of folds the observations or data must be split into without replacement. The model is trained on
k −1 folds and validated on 1 fold, which is repeated k times where the validation fold shifts. This
means that each example is used for training or validation exactly once and the main objective of
this is to develop a generalized model which performs well on unseen data.
k = 10 has been commonly used by related literature for depression analysis and has been found
through experimentation to result in models with low bias and modest variance [27][26][2][4][29].
Therefore, k = 10 was implemented in this work.
For every individual fold, the data was saved for testing and the remaining folds (9 in total) were
saved for training. A model was then fitted to the training set and validating using the test set.
The evaluation score was saved in a confusion matrix, while the model was discarded for every new
iteration. The performance of the algorithm was determined based on evaluation metrics using the
average values of the confusion matrix, including true positives (TP), true negatives (TN), false
positives (FP), and false negative (FN) values.
The cross-validation algorithm of this thesis was specifically designed to make sure that each class
was always represented in each fold and to keep all observations belonging to individual study
participants together. This was done to give a more accurate estimate of performance.
6.4.2
Classifiers
This section presents the theoretical background behind the individual models employed in this
thesis, including the SVM, LR, LDA and k-NN classifiers. Classifiers are critical for the perfor-
mance of algorithms and in previous depression detection studies, these particular classifiers have
been tested extensively. Therefore, for the sake of comparison, they have all been included in this
work. Overall, in previous work, the SVM seem to consistently outperform others and therefore,
more emphasis will be given to the description of this classifier. All the classifiers presented are
supervised machine learning algorithms and rely on the labeled input data (see Appendix B.1).
6.4.2.1
Logistic Regression (LR)
Logistic regression is similar to linear regression and considers the probability of an outcome
belonging to one class or the other, ie. depressed or healthy control, respectively. Instead of fitting
a straight line to the data, logistic regression fits an ”S” shaped logistic function, which predicts
the maximum values (0 or 1) of an observation belonging to either class.
To create the predictive model, logistic regression uses maximum likelihood estimation (MLE) to
obtain the model coeﬀicients that relate the independent variables to the target. Unlike simple
linear regression, LR must be fit iteratively as there is no closed form solution to the classification
problem. Logistic regression was implemented according to the scikit-learn library and applies
regularization by default to avoid overfitting [52].
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
43
==PAGE:55==
6.4.2.2
Linear Discriminant Analysis (LDA)
Linear discriminant analysis is a common tool for classification of two or more classes and assumes
that classes are linearly separable. In the case of two classes, ie. depressed and healthy controls,
LDA uses linear discrimination functions representing a hyperplane to maximally discriminate
between the groups. The hyperplane is created according to the following two criteria:
1. Maximize the distance between means of the two classes.
2. Minimize the variation between each class (within-class variance).
LDA fails when the mean of the distributions are shared but may also be used for dimensionality
reduction and visualization by projecting the data onto the hyperplane and into a lower dimensional
space. LDA was implemented according to the scikit-learn library [52].
6.4.2.3
k-Nearest Neighbors (k-NN)
The k-nearest neighbors algorithm uses proximity to make predictions about the classes to which
individual data points belong. Initially, the algorithm finds the k-nearest data points for every
data point in the training sample. Then, every data point is assigned to the class which is most
frequent among the k-nearest data points. The value of k is a hyperparameter that may affect
performance and in general, as k is reduced towards 1, the model predictions become less stable
and sensitive to outliers. Inversely, as the value is increased, the predictions become more stable
due to majority voting. At some point however, the value of k will become too large and the
ability to catch subtle trends is limited, which decreases the performance. In this work, k = 5 was
selected based on trial and error. The selection was not adopted from previous work, as this value
is typically not specified in the publications (including Wu et al. [36], Cai et al. [2], Hosseinifard
et al. [28] and Acharya et al. [40]). However, Liao et al. [39] and Faust et al. [4] did specify the
k-value and used k = 3 and k = 2, respectively.
The k-NN algorithm was implemented according to the scikit-learn library [52] using default
parameters where all points were weighted equally and the distance between the points were based
on the Euclidean distance.
6.4.2.4
Support Vector Machine (SVM)
The objective of the SVM algorithm is to find a hyperplane in the n-dimensional space (where n is
the number of features) which separates data points into distinctive classes. In particular, the SVM
algorithm is based on the idea of margin maximization where classes of data points are separated
by finding a hyperplane which maximizes the distance between the data points of classes. SVM
algorithms are inherently two-class classifiers but may be extended to multiclass classification by
breaking down the classification problem into multiple binary classification problems.
The hyperplane is a decision boundary that helps classify data points into classes and depends on
the number of features. In principle, when n = 2, the hyperplane is just a line and when n = 3, the
hyperplane becomes a 2-dimensional plane etc. Therefore, it becomes diﬀicult to imagine when the
number of features exceeds 3. For simplicity, the principle of the algorithm for n = 2 is visualized
in Figure 6.5.
The data points closest to the hyperplane are called support vectors and they influence the po-
sition and orientation of the plane. When the data points x are linearly separable, two parallel
hyperplanes H1 and H2 may be selected so that the distance between them is maximized. For a
normalized or standardized dataset, these planes may be presented by
H1 : wT x −b = −1,
(6.16)
H2 : wT x −b = 1,
(6.17)
44
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:56==
Figure 6.5: An optimal decision boundary in SVM classification [4].
where w is the normal vector to the hyperplane and anything on or below H1 is of one class (with
label -1) and anything on or above H2 is of the other class (with label 1). The distance between
these planes is given by
2
||w||, so to maximize the margin, ||w|| should be minimized [57]. In
practical cases however, the data might not be linearly separable and the maximization margin
may be found by solving the following optimization problem
( 1
n
n
$
i=1
max(0, 1 −yi(wT xi −b))) + λ||w||2,
(6.18)
where a training set of {xi, yi}, i = 1, ..., l is presented such that xi ∈Rn are the training points
and yi are the class labels, l is the number of samples, n is the number of features for every
sample and λ > 0 (also called C) determines the trade-off between the maximal margin size and
ensuring that the data point xi is placed on the correct side of the hyperplane. Thus, by solving the
optimization problem presented, ie. by finding the optimal parameters for w and b, a hyperplane is
effectively designed over an n-dimensional input space to distinguish between the classes [4]. The
optimization problem may be solved by various established techniques but will not be covered in
this thesis.
The original SVM algorithm was constructed as a linear classifier but can be made non-linear
by applying a kernel trick. Using the kernel trick, high-dimensional relationships may be found
without actually transforming the data to a higher dimension. The trick is that the kernel function
represents the training data through a set of pairwise similarity comparisons instead of explicitly
applying the transformations. The resulting algorithm is similar, but includes replacing every dot
product by a non-linear kernel function.
Some of the most popular kernel types are the linear kernel, sigmoidal kernel, polynomial kernel,
and radial basis function (RBF) kernel. The RBF kernel (also known as the Gaussian kernel) is
a non-linear kernel that results in a more complex decision boundary and is usually applied for
non-linear dataset. Mahato et al. [32] investigated three different kernel types (sigmoidal, linear
and RBF) and found that the RBF kernel works best for detection of depression. This result is
furthermore equivalent to the findings of other publications including Movahed et al. [29] and
Bairy et al. [41]. Therefore, the SVM algorithm was implemented according to the scikit-learn
library [52] using the RBF kernel and its default parameters.
6.4.3
Performance Evaluation Metrics
For comparison to related work, five common performance metrics were used to evaluate the
performance of the classifiers including accuracy (Acc), sensitivity (Sen), specificity (Spe), precision
(Pre) and F1-score (F1). These metrics were obtained using the output of the final confusion matrix
(average of 100 × 10-fold cross-validation) given by true positives (TP), true negatives (TN), false
positives (FP), and false negative (FN) values. The expressions of these metrics are given by

Chapter 7
Analysis Outcomes
This chapter presents the analysis outcomes of the combination of algorithms presented in the
previous chapters. More specifically, this chapter presents an analysis of traditional features used
for detection of depression, feature selection methods, machine learning classifiers, eye states,
segmentation lengths and wavelet analysis properties, including wavelet features, mother wavelets
and wavelet levels. The first section presents the changes in brain activity between the left and
right hemispheres in depressed patients, as inter-hemispheric asymmetry has been of frequent
interest in studies of depression. The second section considers the use of traditional methods for
identifying depression including state-of-the-art classifiers and feature selection methods based on
temporal and spectral features. The third section presents the outcomes of the wavelet transform
and the importance of the mother wavelets and wavelet decomposition levels for identification of
depression. At last, a final overview of the analyses and proposed findings is provided as well as a
final validation of the best performing algorithms. In this regard, the post-treatment dataset will
be used for validation and an overview of the effect of tDCS will be presented by observing the
overall behavior of the study participants before and after treatment.
7.1
Brain Asymmetry Analysis Outcomes
This section presents the analysis outcomes of investigating the inter-hemispheric asymmetry of
the brain and paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry of six brain regions. In this case, the asymmetry
measures were computed for the frequency bands presented in Table 6.4 and based on standard
FFT-based power estimations using signals of 120 seconds from Dataset A and B in the eyes-closed
and pre-treatment state. For analysis results of Dataset A in the eyes-open state, please refer to
Appendix E.1.
7.1.1
Inter-hemispheric Asymmetry
Figure 7.1 presents inter-hemispheric asymmetry of Dataset A and B within the frequency bands
of the brain (see Table 6.4). The frequency bands of investigation correspond to different cognitive
characteristics and inter-hemispheric asymmetry is computed based on the average log-ratio of the
left and right hemispheric power in accordance with Mahato et al. [32] and Al-Kaysi et al. [5].
In this case, positive asymmetry values represent increased activity of the right hemisphere, while
negative asymmetry values represent increased activity of the left hemisphere.
In Figure 7.1a for Dataset A, it is seen that the inter-hemispheric asymmetry values are positive for
the healthy controls in the majority of the frequency bands. This indicates that healthy controls
exhibit increased activity of the right hemisphere compared to depressed subjects. Furthermore,
the depressed class exhibit overall negative asymmetry values which represent increased activity
of the left hemisphere.
In Figure 7.1b for Dataset B, contrasting findings are presented.
In
this figure, the depressed class present with positive inter-hemispheric asymmetry values in the
majority of frequency bands indicating that the right hemisphere is more active instead of the
left. Furthermore, the healthy controls of Dataset B display significantly smaller average values
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
47
==PAGE:59==
of asymmetry compared to the healthy controls of Dataset A. Overall, the large uncertainty bars
presented in both figures must be considered and suggest that the variation is substantial within
the samples.
(a) Dataset A.
(b) Dataset B.
Figure 7.1: Inter-hemispheric asymmetry of (a) Dataset A and (b) Dataset B in the eyes-closed
and pre-treatment state. The figures present the average log-ratio of left and right hemispheric
power grouped by frequency bands, including all, delta, theta, alpha, beta and gamma bands,
respectively. The uncertainty bar represents a single standard deviation. Positive values represent
increased activity of the right hemisphere and negative values represent increased activity of the left
hemisphere. In (a) the findings suggest that inter-hemispheric asymmetry is negative in depression
subjects, particularly in the all, delta, theta and and alpha frequency bands. In (b) the findings
suggest that inter-hemispheric asymmetry is positive in depression subjects. In both cases, the
high standard deviation of the classes indicate that the variation is substantial within the samples.
7.1.2
Paired Inter-hemispheric Asymmetry
Paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry is based on individual pairs of electrode channels located
at each hemisphere.
Figure 7.2 and 7.3 present paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry based on
electrode channel pairs of brain regions, including the prefrontal, frontal, central, parietal, temporal
and occipital regions. As previously presented, the positive asymmetry values represent increased
activity of the right hemisphere, while negative asymmetry values represent increased activity of
the left hemisphere.
Figure 7.2 presents the paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry of the study participants of Dataset A
and indicate that healthy controls specifically exhibit increased asymmetry in the temporal region
compared to other brain regions. These findings do not include the gamma band where distinctive
differences between the classes are not present in any of the brain regions.

traditional analysis outcomes.

as a result of the top performing classification models for accurate detection of depression. The
initial model parameters of the framework are selected according to common findings of related
literature. For investigation of the proposed methodology presented in this thesis, the parameters
of the framework change accordingly.

Figure 7.5: Investigation of feature selection methods. This figure presents the classification accu-
racies for three feature selection methods grouped by top feature sets. The accuracies presented are
identical to the listed accuracies in Table 7.4 and the uncertainty bar represents a single standard
deviation. It is seen that the mRMR algorithm outperforms the other feature selection methods
and that the ANOVA F-test performs worse compared to the mRMR and SFS algorithms. With
relation to the feature sets, the Combined Asymmetry and Feature Set 1 feature set performs well
for all feature selection methods, in contrast to the Combined Power and Asymmetry feature set
that performs worse than the remaining feature sets.
In Figure 7.6, it is seen that the classification accuracy is dependent on the number of features
selected by the algorithms. In particular, the LDA classifier is more dependent on the number of
features compared to the remaining classifiers and yields higher accuracies for less than 4 features
for classification. The LR and SVM classifiers are more stable and do not increase or decrease
significantly with the number of selected features. The stability of the k-NN classifier seem to
depend on the feature selection algorithm and is very stable for feature selection based on the
ANOVA F-test.

accuracies in Table 7.4. The uncertainty bar represents a single standard deviation. It is seen
that the mRMR algorithm generally outperforms the other feature selection methods and that the
performance of the classifiers is more or less dependent on the number of features selected by the
algorithms. The LR and SVM classifiers are more stable than the remaining classifiers and do not
increase or decrease significantly with the number of selected features.
7.2.3
Investigation of Eye States
This section investigates the significance of the eye states for distinguishing between depressed and
healthy controls. Table 7.5 presents the model parameters used for investigating the eye states and
the results are visualized in Table 7.2, E.4 and E.5. Table 7.2 provides an overview of the results in
the eyes-closed state of Dataset A and may be found in Section 7.2.1, as these results are included
in the investigation of classifiers. The remaining tables are provided in Appendix E.3.
In the tables, the top performing features are based on combinations of feature subsets and there-
fore, the results of the five feature subsets identical to previous analyses are presented in Figure
E.4. In the figure, it is presented that the classification accuracy of Dataset A is highly dependent
on the eye state used for analysis compared to Dataset B. For Dataset A, the eyes-open state pro-
vides the lowest performances across the selected feature set, while the eyes-open state for Dataset
B outperforms the eyes-closed state in some cases. The highest classification performance is seen
for the Combination of Asymmetry and Feature set 3 feature set and presents the accuracy of 95.60
± 1.21%, also identified in previous analyses.

7.2.4.1
Temporal Region Analysis
In Table 7.7 and 7.8, the results of 100 × 10-fold cross-validation based on 6 temporal electrode
channels and parameters proposed by previous findings are presented for Dataset A and B, respec-
tively. Segmentation lengths include 2, 10, 60 and 120 s using the top feature sets identified in the
previous sections. The highest classification accuracy is seen for 120 s and 60 s based on Dataset
A (93.04 ± 2.88% and 92.60 ± 1.87%, respectively) and 120 s and 60 s based on Dataset B (85.41
± 1.96% and 85.23 ± 1.92%, respectively).
To visualize trends, the classification accuracies of Table 7.7 and 7.8 are presented in Figure 7.8.
In Figure 7.8, it is seen that the classification accuracy for Dataset A and B in most cases increases
with increasing segmentation length. This tendency is particularly evident for Dataset A, where
the accuracy of the model for the Combined Power and Feature Set 2 feature set improves from
61.18 ± 1.56% to 84.56 ± 2.73% using segments of 2 and 120 s, respectively. For both datasets
however, the difference of using 60 s in contrast to 120 s does not seem significant. Furthermore,
when considering the individual feature set, the Combined Power and Asymmetry presents similar
performance results for both datasets according to the segmentation lengths of investigation.
Figure 7.8: Investigation of segmentation lengths based on 6 electrodes from the temporal region.
This figure presents the classification accuracies for varying the segmentation lengths of Dataset A
and B and is grouped by the top feature sets. The accuracies presented are identical to the listed
accuracies in Table 7.7 and 7.8 for Dataset A and B, respectively. The uncertainty bar represents
a single standard deviation. It is seen that the classification accuracy for Dataset A and B in most
cases increases with increasing segmentation lengths. For all the feature sets of interest, Dataset
B increases less than Dataset A. Furthermore, it is seen that using 60 s in contrast to 120 s does
not significantly affect the performances for the individual datasets.
7.2.4.2
Frontal Region Analysis
The tables providing the classification outcomes of the analysis of the frontal electrode channels
and segmentation lengths is provided in Table E.7 and E.8 in Appendix E.4. In Table E.7 and
E.8, the results of 100 × 10-fold cross-validation using 3 frontal electrode channels and parameters
proposed by previous findings are presented for Dataset A and B, respectively.
Segmentation lengths include 2, 10, 60 and 120 s for the top feature sets identified in the previous
sections and the highest classification accuracy is seen for 60 s and 120 s based on Dataset A (75.86
± 2.55% and 74.16 ± 2.37%, respectively) and for 120 s and 10 s based on Dataset B (83.69 ±
1.81% and 82.85 ± 1.80%, respectively). For evaluation of additional performance metrics, please
refer to Appendix E.4.

Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:70==
Figure 7.9 presents an overview of the accuracies of both datasets for all segmentation lengths. In
the figure, it is seen that the performances for the majority of the features are greater for Dataset
B compared to Dataset A. Furthermore, the performances seem to increase with segmentation
lengths for Dataset A, whereas this tendency is not visible for the findings of Dataset B. In this
regard, it is clear that Dataset A requires more data to identify depression than Dataset B. The
performances for Dataset B suggest that this dataset is independent of the segmentation lengths
and perform well for all lengths of investigation. Furthermore, the feature set Combined Asymmetry
and Feature Set 1 seem to generalize better between the two datasets but present with some of the
lowest performances.
Figure 7.9: Investigation of segmentation lengths based on 3 electrodes from the frontal region.
This figure presents the classification accuracies for varying the segmentation lengths of Dataset A
and B and is grouped by the top feature sets. The accuracies presented are identical to the listed
accuracies in Table E.7 and E.8 for Dataset A and B, respectively. The uncertainty bar represents
a single standard deviation. It is seen that the performances of most feature sets are greater for
Dataset B compared to Dataset A. For Dataset A, the performances also increase according to the
segmentation lengths of interest, whereas the performances for Dataset B remain consistent for all
segmentation lengths.
7.2.4.3
Optimal Set of Electrode Channels
As the analysis of the frontal and temporal region suggest that the segmentation length of 120
s performs well for distinguishing between depressed and healthy controls in both datasets, this
segmentation length is used for evaluating the optimal set of electrode channels used by related
work (ie. 3, 6 and 19 electrodes).
Figure 7.10 presents the results of these findings presented in Table 7.2 (DA: 19), E.5 (DB: 19),
7.7 (DA: 6), 7.8 (DB: 6), E.7 (DA: 3) and E.8 (DB: 3), and is based on the parameters proposed
by previous sections, including 100 × 10-fold cross-validation based on SVM classification, mRMR
feature selection, the eyes-closed state and 120 s of Dataset A and B.
In Figure 7.10, it is seen that the classification performances increase with the number of electrode
channels. The highest performances are obtained with 19 electrode channels for both datasets but
is followed by the results obtained using 6 electrode channels. For example, the performance is
slightly better in the Combined Asymmetry and Feature Set 1 case using 6 electrode channels. In
the figure, it is also seen that the classification accuracies are significantly higher for most feature
set based on Dataset A compared to Dataset B using 19 and 6 channels. However, an exception is
made for the cases based on 3 electrode channels, where the opposite effect is found.

for Diagnosis and Treatment Response

Figure 7.10: Investigation of optimal set of electrode channels. This figure presents the classifica-
tion accuracies for varying the number of electrode channels and is grouped by top feature sets.
The data used for analysis is based on 120 s of the eyes-closed data for Dataset A and B. The
accuracies presented are identical to the listed accuracies presented in Section 7.2.1, 7.2.4.2 and
7.2.4.1. The uncertainty bar represents a single standard deviation. It is seen that the classifica-

by employing 19 electrode channels for Dataset A and B and it is seen that Dataset A generally
provides higher classification accuracies compared to Dataset B using 19 and 6 electrode channels.
7.3
Wavelet Analysis Outcomes
This section presents the outcomes related to the analysis of wavelet methodology proposed by re-
lated literature and is organized into three principal investigations including an analysis of wavelet
features, mother wavelets and wavelet levels. Throughout these analyses, the optimal set of pa-
rameters will be determined as a result of the best performing classification models for accurate
detection of depression. The initial model parameters of the wavelet model are selected according
to the findings in Section 7.2 and in relation to the common wavelet findings presented by related
literature. For investigation of the proposed methodology, the model parameters of the models are
changed accordingly.
7.3.1
Investigation of Wavelet Features
Table 7.9 presents the model parameters used for investigating wavelet features presented in this
thesis and the results are visualized in Table 7.10 and 7.11 for Dataset A and B, respectively.
Table 7.9: An overview of the model parameters used for investigation of wavelet features.

features

The initial model was based on a framework proposed by Mumtaz et al. [27] using the Db4 mother
wavelet and 5-level wavelet decomposition according the the frequency bands of the brain (ie. via
detail and approximate wavelet signals D1 −D5 and A5).
Table 7.10: 100 × 10-fold cross-validation performance of extracted EEG features based on SVM
(RBF kernel) classification and mRMR feature selection for discriminating depressed patients and
healthy controls. The framework was evaluated for 1-5, 10, 15 and 19 features and the performance
of the best performing feature count is presented for each feature set. The highest performance
is outlined in bold and the uncertainty values represent a single standard deviation. The analysis
include signals from 5-level wavelet decomposition using the Db4 mother wavelet. The data used

individual wavelet features across the datasets, the performances of a selection of the features with

presents with high performances across the majority of features. In this case however, Skewness

accuracy of 66.88 ± 3.77% (see Table 7.10).

7.3.3
Investigation of Wavelet Levels
To evaluate the significance of the wavelet levels for classification into healthy and depressed classes
compared to traditional methodology proposed by related literature, a framework was employed
according to Table 7.14. Initially, the significance of individual wavelet levels is evaluated based
on the proposed mother wavelet Db4 and afterwards, pair-wise wavelet levels are investigated
accordingly. In this regard, only analyses based on Dataset A was included and will be evaluated
in terms of related work conducted by Mumtaz et al. [27] using Dataset B.
Table 7.14: An overview of the model parameters used for investigation of wavelet levels.
Model Parameters
Dataset:
A (pre-treatment)
Electrode channels:
19
Eye state:
EC
Segment lengths:
120 s
Features:
Wavelet analysis
feature set 1-3
Feature selection:
mRMR
Classification:
SVM (RBF kernel)
Mother wavelets:
Db4
Wavelet levels:
D1-D5, A5
7.3.3.1
Individual Wavelet Levels
The results of investigating the importance of individual wavelet levels are presented in Table
E.9 in Appendix E.5.1.
In the table, wavelet level D4 involving the frequencies band of 8-16
Hz presents the highest classification performance with an accuracy of 90.60 ± 1.92%. Wavelet
level A5 involving the frequencies band of 0.5-4 Hz also provides a high classification performance
including an accuracy of 90.56 ± 2.17%. The worst classification performance was obtained for D1
involving the frequencies band of 64-70 Hz and presented an accuracy of 60.20 ± 2.57%.
Figure 7.13 presents an overview of the classification accuracies obtained for all wavelet levels and
features investigated. In Figure 7.13, the results of the feature set based on the original signal of
investigation were additionally presented and involves all the frequencies from 0.5 Hz to 70 Hz.
In this regard, it is seen that the feature set evaluated on the broadband signal outperform the
majority of the same feature set based on the individual wavelet levels. However, the feature set
based on the A5 wavelet signal involving the lowest frequencies of the original signal, outperform
the same feature set based on the original signal for all feature set combinations and the Feature
Set 2 feature set.
Overall, the individual Feature Set 1, 2 and 3 generally present the lowest
performances with an exception of Feature Set 1, where the broadband signal and wavelet levels
D4 and D5 distinguish between depressed and healthy controls with accuracies > 84%.

Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
