Figure 7.13: Investigation of individual wavelet levels. This figure presents the classification ac-
curacies for the original broadband signal and separate wavelet levels (D1 −D5 and A5) and is
grouped by individual and combinations of wavelet feature sets presented from the original and
wavelet signals of Dataset A using eyes-closed data and 19 electrode channels. The accuracies
presented are identical to the listed accuracies presented in Table E.9 in Appendix E.5.1 and the
uncertainty bar represents a single standard deviation. It is seen that the original broadband sig-
nal (including frequencies of 0.5-70 Hz) generally outperform the individual wavelet signals, except
wavelet level A5 involving the lowest frequencies of the original signal and corresponds to the delta
frequency band.
7.3.3.2
Wavelet Level Pairs
The results of evaluating wavelet level pairs are presented in Table E.10 in Appendix E.5.2. In
Table E.10, wavelet level pairs including D1 were excluded in accordance with the previous section,
as the feature set based on this signal provided the lowest classification performances. In the table,
the pair-wise combinations of wavelet levels were investigated based on individual feature set. The
best performance was found for the wavelet level pair (A5, D4) and presents an accuracy of 93.04
± 2.17% based on Feature Set 1. To get an overview of these performances, the accuracy metric
of all wavelet combinations is visualized in Figure 7.14.
Figure 7.14: Investigation of pair-wise wavelet levels according to Feature Set 1, 2 and 3. This
figure presents the classification accuracies for pair-wise combinations of D1 −D5 and A5 wavelet
signals and is grouped by individual wavelet feature sets. The data is based on 5-level decomposi-
tion of Dataset A using eyes-closed data and 19 electrode channels. The accuracies presented are
identical to the listed accuracies presented in Table E.10 in Appendix E.5.2 and the uncertainty
bar represents a single standard deviation. It is seen that the statistical features of Feature Set 1
outperform the remaining feature sets and presents accuracies above 90% for the pair-wise combi-
nations of (A5, D4), (D2, D4), (D2, D5) and (D4, D5). The wavelet level pair (A5, D4) seem to
provide the best accuracies across all individual feature set.
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
65
==PAGE:77==
In Figure 7.14, Feature Set 1, including the statistical features, seem to present a better combination
of features for detection of depression. In this regard, the pair-wise combinations of (A5, D4), (D2,
D4), (D2, D5) and (D4, D5) present accuracies above 90%. In the case of (D3, D4), accuracies
of 89.76±2.56% and 91.68±2.25% are presented for Feature Set 1 and 3, respectively. The wavelet
level pair (A5, D4) seem to provide the best accuracies across all individual feature set.
7.4
Validation of the Algorithms
An overview of the analyses and findings presented in Section 7.2 and 7.3 is visualized in Figure 7.15.
Based on these findings, the top classification performances for distinguishing between depressed
and healthy controls were selected for validation of the post-treatment dataset of Dataset A. For
classification into depressed and healthy controls, the features selected for the top classification
performances were extracted and labels changed according to the psychometric test scores provided
in Table B.1 (see Appendix B.1).
The features selected for the top pre-treatment models are
presented in Table 7.15 and 7.16 for traditional and wavelet analysis, respectively.
Table 7.15 presents the features selected for the top five classification performances and involves
2 to 5 features per model. The Paired temporal asymmetry (All) feature is selected in all cases
which include combined asymmetry features and electrode channels Fp2-A2 and/or TP8-A2 seem
to appear in the majority of the models.

7.4.1
Treatment Response
For an evaluation of the effect of tDCS therapy and overall behavior of the study participants of
Dataset A before and after treatment, the evolution of selected features based on the provided pre-
and post-treatment datasets were evaluated and presented in Figure 7.16.
Figure 7.16a displays the pre- and post-treatment values of paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry
in the frontal area of the brain based on the delta frequency band (0.5-4 Hz), while figure 7.16b
displays the pre- and post-treatment values of paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry in the temporal
area of the brain (based on all frequency components of the original signal).
In both figures,
the depressed subjects are presented according to the sham and active groups. Furthermore, by
considering the psychometric test scores provided in Table B.1 (see Appendix B.1) additional
subgroups including ’responders’ were identified as depressed subjects who became healthy after
treatment and ’non-responders’ as depressed subjects who did not become healthy after treatment.
In Figure 7.16a, the active and sham groups have been marked according to their respective
subgroups. Overall, the asymmetry values are elevated after treatment and do not seem to reveal
any tendencies of either of the groups. Equivalent findings are presented in Figure 7.16b.
(a) Paired Frontal Asymmetry (Delta).
(b) Paired Temporal Asymmetry (All).
Figure 7.16: Investigation of treatment response for depressed subjects. This figure presents the
pre- and post-treatment response of features including Paired Frontal Asymmetry (Delta) in Figure
7.16a and Paired Temporal Asymmetry (All) in Figure 7.16b for study participants in the sham
and active groups (total sham: 2, total active: 7). In the figures, the non-responders (in red) are
still depressed subsequent to treatment with tDCS (active or sham), while responders (in black)
have become healthy after treatment with tDCS. It is seen that the asymmetry values are generally
elevated after treatment in both subgroups and that there is no clear differential trend between
the groups.
In Figure 7.17, the values of both features in the pre- and post-treatment state is visualized to
display the movement of the depressed and healthy controls as a result of treatment. In Figure
7.17a for the healthy controls, it is seen that these subjects move away from their initial starting
point subsequent to treatment with tDCS. The movement of the subjects as a result of treatment
seem unsystematic and random and all subjects remain healthy. The depressed subjects receiving
active tDCS treatment are displayed in Figure 7.17b. In the figure, it is seen that approx. 70%
become healthy subsequent to treatment, which may indicate that tDCS does have an effect on
depression although the datasets contains too few participants to make conclusions with statistical
certainty.
In Figure 7.17b, it is also seen that the directions of the active subjects seem less random than
for the healthy controls, and seem to present with increasing asymmetry subsequent to treatment.
For the sham group in Figure 7.17c, this is also the case, although none of the two subjects become
healthy. In Figure 7.17d, the healthy and depressed classes are visualised in one figure and do not
reveal particular tendencies in either of the groups to help differentiate the classes.

Chapter 8
Discussion
This chapter interprets the principal research findings of this thesis and the methodologies included
for achieving these. A discussion on the significance of the research findings and their implications
will likewise be included and evaluated in terms of previous studies and literature. Furthermore,
the remaining answers to the research questions presented in this thesis will be provided as well as
a description on future research directions.
8.1
Research Findings
As the research findings of this thesis are compared to related work throughout this section, it
should be noted that a direct comparison of the previous findings to the findings presented in
this thesis may be biased and imprecise, as the variability in terms of data acquisition protocols
and methodologies used for detection of depression is high. Nevertheless, these comparisons may
inspire future research directions and provide critical insight and worthwhile discussions on the
subject of depression analysis.
Scope. To address the issue of variability, a second dataset was presented in this thesis to evaluate
the effect of the methodologies for detection of depression and the generalization ability of the
models based on independent datasets. This was done to accurately compare the performance
of datasets and determine the clinical utility of the methods proposed by related literature. As
depression is a highly heterogeneous disorder, it has proven diﬀicult to accurately identify the
mechanisms of depression in EEG signals. These signals are often limited in size and presented in
dataset of few study participants. Therefore, the state-of-the-art findings of depression biomarkers
have been widely studied and include many inconsistencies evaluated in this thesis. As methodolog-
ical variability and inconsistencies are present within automatic identification of clinical depression,
it is unknown whether such methods based on EEG signal processing and machine learning may be
able to accurately identify clinical depression based on different variations of data. In this regard,
the findings of this thesis are presented accordingly.
Inter-hemispheric Asymmetry. In depression analysis, inter-hemispheric asymmetry (specifically
alpha asymmetry in the frontal region) has been of great interest for researchers for years but
presents with inconsistent findings.
Therefore, inter-hemispheric asymmetry and paired inter-
hemispheric asymmetry was evaluated and presented differential results for depressed and healthy
individuals.
In the case of Dataset A, specifically paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry of the
temporal region was identified, but did not seem to generalize well for Dataset B (see Figure 7.2
and 7.3). As a result, inter-hemispheric asymmetry and paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry may
present with little diagnostic capability and is highly dependent on the dataset of interest and
sets of electrode channels. This was also indicated in the exploratory analysis performed prior
to these definitive findings presented in Appendix C. In relation to the classification performance
on the individual dataset, paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry outperformed inter-hemispheric
asymmetry and provided average classification accuracies from 75.08% to 87.02% based on 100
permutation tests for both datasets in the eyes-open and eyes-closed state (see Table 7.2, E.5
and E.4). As presented in Table 3.2, state-of-the-art findings of asymmetry in depressed subjects
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
71
==PAGE:83==
include increased right frontal cortex activation and decreased left frontal cortex activation in the
alpha frequency band [31]. However, this was not found for either dataset of investigation and as
emphasized by Wu et al. [36], inconsistencies of asymmetry findings are many and the effectiveness
of frontal alpha asymmetry for classification of depression is therefore still inconclusive. In this
thesis, combinations of channels across different brain areas were not investigated.
Classifiers.
In relation to the classifiers of investigation, the SVM classifier with RBF kernel
achieved the best performances compared to the other classifiers (LDA, LR and k-NN). Additional
classifiers including the SVM (LIN), RF and MLP were also investigated but did not outperform
the SVM (RBF) classifier. Movahed et al. [29] similarly investigated other classifiers (SVM (LIN),
SVM (RBF), LR, DT, NB, RB, GB, RF) and found that the SVM (RBF) classifier outperformed
the remaining classifiers. This was also established by Mahato et al. [32] based on an investigation
of three kernels of SVM. Mumtaz et al. [26] investigated the LR, NB and SVM (LIN) classifiers
and found the highest performance using the SVM classifier. As Cai et al. [2] found the best
classification result using the k-NN classifier based on 3 frontal electrode channels, the k-NN was
also included in this study. In this case, the authors performed a comparison of four classification
methods including the SVM (RBF), k-NN, DT and ANN.
As presented by Cai et al. [2], the SVM classifier is advantageous and particularly effective in high
dimensional spaces. This classifier has solved the issue of non-linear classification based on small
sample data by constructing a decision surface (or an infinite-dimensional space) using a non-linear
kernel function. The SVM is not suitable for large data sets however, as the complexity of the
algorithm is highly dependent on the size. For larger datasets, deep learning has proven highly
useful in fields such as image classification, natural language processing, and speech recognition
by progressively extracting higher-level features from the dataset. For deep learning approaches in
depression analysis however, the inadequacy of input data is a major issue and has provided serious
issues of overfitting and unreliable accuracies [58]. Researchers have presented several approaches to
overcome these issues such as implementing windowing methods and sample generation to produce
dataset of substantial sizes [58]. Nevertheless, these issues remain of primary concern and must be
considered for accurate detection of depression with high sensitivity.
Feature Selection. Of the feature selection methods of investigation, the mRMR algorithm provided
the best results based on segmentation lengths of 120 seconds. The classification performances
of the feature set were also evaluated for the remaining classifiers (LDA, LR, k-NN) but solely
presented according to the performances by the SVM classifier in Table 7.4. The performances
of the remaining classifiers were visualized in Figure 7.5 for evaluation of the importance of the
number of features selected for the algorithms.
Here it was found that the mRMR algorithm
generally outperforms the other feature selection methods and that the SVM classifier generally
provides the best classification result independent of the number of features selected by the feature
selection algorithms. Cai et al. [2] likewise presented the mRMR algorithm for feature selection,
while Mumtaz et al. [27] found that sequential backward selection achieves better classification
results. In this study, rank-based feature selection was implemented based on sequential forward
selection and findings presented lower classification performances based on the feature sets of
investigation. In this regard, it must be acknowledged that both the mRMR and the SFS algorithm
may underestimate the usefulness of features as they are unable to capture significant interactions
between features which can increase relevancy.
This may lead to reduced performances when
the features are individually useless, but useful when combined. Nevertheless, as EEG signals
typically involve redundant and/or irrelevant features that may be highly correlated or does not
affect the classification performance, feature selection is commonly adapted to select a subset of
relevant features, reduce the dimension of the feature space or computational load, and improve
the classification performance.
Features. In this thesis, state-of-the-art features proposed by previous studies and literature were
evaluated to determine the generalization ability of the features based on independent dataset
providing variability in the system. The findings of this thesis suggest that the feature sets based
on a combination of linear and/or non-linear measures, including asymmetry and temporal/wavelet
features, provide better classification performances compared to feature sets based on individual
linear and non-linear measures. Therefore, top feature sets were identified to evaluate how the
variability in terms of methodologies affect proposed state-of-the-art machine learning methods.
72
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:84==
The highest classification performance presented in this thesis presents a classification accuracy
of 95.60 ± 1.21% for the Combined Asymmetry & Feature Set 3 feature set and based on the
eyes-closed and pre-treatment state of Dataset A (see Table 7.2). In comparison to the eyes-closed
state, the eyes-open state presented an accuracy of 79.68 ± 2.18 % for the same feature set (see
Table E.4). In the case of Dataset B provided in Table E.5, the results are 86.67 ± 1.94% and 82.37
± 1.79% in the eyes-closed and eyes-open state, respectively. The Combined Asymmetry & Feature
Set 3 feature set seems to provide a good generalization ability and contains linear and non-linear
features including energy, Hurst exponent and Hjorth mobility & complexity besides the measures
of inter-hemispheric asymmetry and paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry. Using a combination of
linear and non-linear features was likewise proposed by Movahed et al.[29], Mumtaz et al. [26]
and Faust et al. [4], and implemented by the majority of related literature. Movahed et al.[29]
specifically achieves one of the best performances described at present time based on conventional
machine learning models. In this study, the authors implemented the proposed method on Dataset
B provided by Mumtaz et al. [27] and performed validation using the MODMA dataset [33]. On the
dataset provided by Mumtaz et al., the proposed algorithm outperformed other machine learning
approaches of classification and achieved the highest accuracy of 99.0% with sensitivity of 98.4% and
specificity of 99.6% based on the SVM (RBF) classifier and using 10-fold cross-validation. When
applied on the MODMA dataset, the SVM (LIN) provided the best classification performance
and obtained an average accuracy of 97.3% with sensitivity of 98.3% and specificity of 97.0%.
Therefore, this model provides the most optimal algorithm of all the available automatic methods
for identification of clinical depression in terms of both model performance and generalization
ability.
The algorithm proposed by Movahed et al. [29] differs from the algorithms presented in this thesis
by a number of ways, but specifically the authors used a combination of feature sets including
statistical, spectral, wavelet, nonlinear and functional connectivity measures.
The authors do
not specify the final selection of features but observed that functional connectivity and spectral
features had the largest share of the final feature matrix, while statistical and wavelet features had
the least share. The functional connectivity of the brain was quantified based on synchronization
likelihood, and was not implemented in this thesis. Furthermore, it is worth mentioning that the
authors visually inspected all of the EEG signals prior to analysis for eliminating all noisy intervals
left subsequent to ICA artifact removal. This step was not performed in this thesis and instead
individuals presenting with significant noise levels were excluded.
Eye States. A study on the importance of eye states for depression analysis has not been provided
by related literature and was therefore evaluated in this thesis. Movahed et al. [29], Mumtaz et
al. [46][27][26] and Acharya et al.[40] used a combination of eyes-open and eyes-closed data for
analysis, while Cai et al. [2], Hosseinifard et al. [28], Lebiecka et al. [59] and Mahato et al. [32]
provided their findings based on eyes-closed data. Furthermore, Faust et al. [4] and Puthankattil
et al. [49] used the same eyes-open dataset for analysis of depression. The findings of this thesis
on the significance of the eye states for classification of depressed and healthy controls suggest
that eyes-closed and eyes-open data provide similar performances. This was interpreted as the
eyes-closed state provided better results for Dataset A, while the eyes-open state provided slightly
better results for some feature sets compared to the eyes-closed state for Dataset B. Likewise,
findings of related work suggest that high classification performances are achievable in either case
and may not influence the analysis significantly. Mumtaz et al. [27] provided topographic maps for
depressed female and male study participants and found that during both eyes-closed and eyes-open
conditions, the frontal and temporal areas were significantly different between the groups.
As the performances of algorithms are highly influenced by the amount of artifacts present in the
signals and whether these have been properly excluded prior to analysis, artifacts introduced by
the eyes may have influenced the final classification performance. This may explain the results for
Dataset A, as more artifacts were identified in the eyes-open state compared to the eyes-closed state
due to eye-blinks. Although ICA was performed for removal of eye-blinks during pre-processing of
the signals, the signals were not inspected afterwards to remove any remaining noise components.
By visual inspection, it should also be acknowledged that higher levels of noise were present in
Dataset A compared to Dataset B, which may have affected the research findings of this thesis.
An analysis of using a combination of eyes-open and eyes-closed data was not considered in this
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
73
==PAGE:85==
work but would provide with additional information on the significance of the eye states.
As
algorithms must be built to maintain performance with different variations of data however, using
a combination of eyes-open and eyes-closed data must be considered for future work. In the case
of this thesis, eyes-closed data was used for analysis of the methodology due to overall better
performances and lower noise levels.
Segmentation Lengths. In depression analysis, the size of datasets are typically small and include
signals with lengths of two to five minutes per study participant in the eyes-closed and/or eyes-
open state. The methodology of analyzing these signals differ and specifically, no standard has
been made in terms of segmentation lengths applied for analysis which may have affected the
findings of literature. Therefore, an analysis of optimal segmentation lengths was provided in this
thesis. Specifically, Mahato et al. [32] used segments of 2 s for analysis, whereas other studies
such as Movahed et al. [29] investigated segments of 60 s, 120 s and 300 s. Movahed et al. [29]
obtained the highest classification performance using 60 s, while Mumtaz et al [27] found slightly
better results for 120 s compared to segments of 60 s or 180 s. In this thesis, segments of 2 s, 10
s, 60 s and 120 s were investigated based on the findings of related literature and including six
electrode channels of the temporal region and three electrodes of the frontal region. These subsets
of channels were selected as proposed by Mahato et al.[32] and Cai et al. [2] and furthermore as
it was hypothesized that a lower number of electrode channels would be more susceptible to the
lengths applied by segmentation. In this thesis, the findings suggest that 120 s segments provides
better classification performances for both datasets in the case of frontal and temporal electrode
channels. The performances vary with each dataset and specifically Dataset A was more dependent
of the segmentation lengths, presenting with increasing classification performances for increasing
segmentation lengths compared to Dataset B, where no significant differences are visible between
the segmentation lengths of interest. In the case of the temporal electrode channels, it may be
indicated that segmentation lengths of 60 s and 120 s perform slightly better than 2 s and 10 s. This
may be due to the intrinsic nature of EEG signals which exhibit complex behavior with nonlinear
dynamic properties and contain the heterogeneous and dynamical properties of depression which
may be revealed based on increasing segmentation lengths.
In the case of classification performances, the findings based on 120 s were presented for the analysis
of 3, 6 and 19 electrode channels. The highest performances were obtained for 19 electrode channels
in both dataset and were notably different from the results obtained for 6 and 3 electrode channels in
majority of feature sets. Some feature sets however, did not show significant performance differences
between employing 19 and 6 electrode channels. In relation to findings of related literature, Cai
et al. achieved the highest accuracy of 79.27% based on three electrode channels, while Mahato
et al. achieved the highest accuracy of 96.02% based on six electrode channels. It must be noted
that no other performance metrics were provided by the authors and that different samples of the
same participants were likely included in both training and test sets at the same. Although this
classification scheme may lead to overfitting, these findings suggest that performances increase
with the number of electrodes but may not require all channels for successful identification of
depression. It was also presented that Dataset B provided significantly higher classification results
compared to Dataset A based on 3 electrode channels. The opposite effect was presented for 6 and
19 electrode channels and may have been affected by the levels of artifacts in either dataset or a
poor generalization ability of the features from these channels.
Wavelet Analysis. The findings of wavelet analysis provided the highest classification accuracy of
95.24 ± 1.58 % for the Feature Set 1 & 2 feature set and included statistical and non-linear features
based on four electrode channels including electrodes from the frontal and temporal region (see
Table 6.3). These findings were obtained for the Db4 mother wavelet in accordance with Mumtaz et
al. [27] and features computed from the signals D2−D5 and A5 were selected by the mRMR feature
selection algorithm. Mumtaz et al. [27] similarly extracted wavelet features from the frontal and
temporal brain regions using Dataset B and found the highest classification performances based on
the Db4 mother wavelet. The selection of this mother wavelet was based on the highest classification
accuracy when compared to other mother wavelets, but the mother wavelets of investigation were
not specified. The study involved 5-level wavelet decomposition into the established frequencies of
the brain and involved 100 × 10-fold cross-validation for classification into depressed and healthy
controls.
In the study, the authors presented the highest classification accuracy of 89.6 ± 5.1
74
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:86==
% based on rank-based feature selection. The study also evaluated features based on the STFT
and EMD (empirical mode decomposition) and found that the wavelet transform provided the
highest performance.
The study particularly found that the delta and theta frequency bands
of wavelet decomposition (D5, A5) presented higher eﬀiciencies in discriminating between the
classes of interest. In this study, wavelet levels were investigated individually and pair-wise for
19 electrode channels. As a result of investigating individual wavelet levels, D4 and A5 provided
the best classification performances, while the D1 wavelet level provided the worst classification
performances (where D4, A5 and D1 levels correspond to the alpha, delta and gamma frequency
bands). Similarly, Mumtaz et al. [27] and Movahed et al. [29] et al. found that the theta and
delta frequency bands are of relevance, while Mumtaz et al. [26] also found that the alpha band
is significant. Both supporting and contrasting findings exist in related literature, and therefore,
all frequency bands of the brain have remained of interest for distinguishing between depressed
and healthy controls [44][3]. In a review study by by Milena Radenkovic [3], it was also presented
that broadband analysis has been shown to be more successful than dividing the signals into
frequency bands. Therefore, the findings of the individual wavelet levels were compared to features
extracted from the original signal of interest containing all frequency components. In this regard,
findings suggest that the equivalent features of the original signal outperform the majority of the
same feature sets based on the individual wavelet levels. However, when considering all combined
feature sets, the A5 wavelet level does outperform the original broadband signal.
It must be
acknowledged that the broadband signal must be compared to features of several wavelet levels for
a fair comparison, but this was not presented in this thesis. A pair-wise investigation of individual
wavelet levels presented that significant wavelet levels were highly dependent on the feature set of
interest. The best performance was found for the wavelet level pair (A5, D4) and presented an
accuracy of 93.04±2.17% based on Feature Set 1. When evaluating the features individually for
Dataset A and B, it was seen that the Hurst exponent presented the highest accuracy for Dataset A,
while Higuchi’s fractal dimension provided the highest accuracy for Dataset B. The Hurst exponent
however, provided the highest performance based on both dataset and presented an accuracy of
91.45 ± 2.04% for Dataset A and 86.87 ± 2.28% for Dataset B. As the Hurst exponent shows a
high ability to generalize between independent dataset, these findings support related literature
in the fact that non-linear measures may be particularly important for the analysis of depression.
The relevance of the Hurst exponent for detection of depression was likewise presented by Acharya
et al. [40] and Bairy et al. [41] and relates to the autocorrelations of the signal and is a measure
of long-term memory. In this thesis, the Hurst exponent was included in both Feature Set 2 and
Feature Set 3 but was only represented once in the final wavelet model presenting the highest
classification accuracy of 95.24 ± 1.58% (see Table 7.13). A shortfall of the analysis was that the
generalization of this wavelet model, including the mother wavelet and decomposition levels, were
not evaluated based on Dataset B prior to validation on post-treatment data. Therefore, an analysis
must be conducted to establish whether changing the hyperparameters such as mother wavelet and
decomposition levels provides better models for generalizing between different variations of data.
Validation of Models. For validation of the models, the models presenting the highest performance
on the pre-treatment datasets were selected and evaluated on the post-treatment dataset. This
data was treated as another independent dataset as proposed by Movahed et al. [29] and included
both depressed and healthy study participants based on updated labels from the DASS-21 scores
presented in Table B.1 in Appendix. In the post-treatment dataset five additional study partici-
pants were included from Dataset A and consisted of 15 depressed and 15 healthy individuals. The
features selected for the pre-treatment models were selected for evaluation on the post-treatment
models and the same framework was used for training and testing the dataset. As presented in
Table 7.17, the post-treatment model achieved accuracies of approximately 61% for all of the tra-
ditional models and indicated that the generalization ability of the models was very low. The same
was achieved for the model proposed in wavelet analysis and suggests that depression is diﬀicult
to accurately identify across multiple datasets based on models with pre-specified features and
channels. In the case of evaluating the models using Dataset B, feature selection was also per-
formed to evaluate the fundamental machine learning methodology and this may have increased
the performance accuracies of these models significantly.
Treatment Response. For estimating the treatment response of tDCS, paired temporal and frontal
inter-hemispheric asymmetry features were identified according the models presented in the analysis
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
75
==PAGE:87==
of traditional methodologies. However, as presented for validation of the models, the features for
evaluating the treatment response were not properly established to accurately visualize the overall
behavior of the study participants before and after treatment. This was especially indicated in
Figure 7.17, showing the unsystematic movements of the study participants in the feature space.
However, as presented by Table B.1 in Appendix B.1, 5 of 7 of the active study participants included
in this study across the pre- and post-datasets do respond to treatment with tDCS. A general
analysis of the treatment response to tDCS would also have been possible with an algorithm which
accurately distinguishes between depressed and healthy controls across all independent dataset.
However, such a model was not achieved in this thesis and is required prior to direct evaluation of
the treatment response.
In this thesis, it was also considered whether a prediction of tDCS treatment response was achiev-
able based on the work presented by Al-Kaysi et al. [5] and others [7][32][27], as it is commonly
known that a sizable percentage of individuals suffering from depression remain non-responsive or
poorly responsive to therapy. Thus, this would enable an identification of study participants re-
sponding to treatment with tDCS prior to actually receiving tDCS therapy and therefore it would
save valuable time for both staff and individuals suffering from depression. Furthermore, it would
provide for a faster adoption of tDCS therapy into clinical settings as it has yet to be approved
by the FDA as an effective and safe treatment. Al-Kaysi et al. [5] demonstrated the feasibility of
predicting tDCS treatment outcomes based on resting state EEG data by accurately predicting the
treatment response in 8 out of 10 participants based on mood scores similar to the DASS-21 scores
provided in this thesis. However, due to the size of Dataset A, presenting with 7 depressed study
participants receiving active treatment with tDCS across the pre- and post-treatment dataset, this
was not investigated and must be considered for future work.
Classification. As proposed by Mumtaz et al. [26][27][16][46] 100 permutation tests with 10-fold
cross-validation may be used to improve the robustness of underlying machine learning models.
Therefore, this approach was employed in this thesis. The small number of study participants
in the datasets presented in this thesis also provided some limitations in terms of evaluating the
generalization ability of algorithms and as a result, the cross-validation scheme was important
to further reduce overfitting. As emphasized by Wu et al. [36], previous studies have also been
limited in relation to the size of datasets and as a result, the cross-validation scheme often suffered
from overfitting due to information and data leakage. As depression is furthermore known as a
heterogeneous disorder, datasets of limited size may encounter even greater diﬀiculties to accurately
identify depression, while considering the inherent nature of the signals and natural variability. To
overcome this, cross-validation was specifically designed to make sure that each class was always
represented in each fold and to keep all observations belonging to individual study participants
together. This was done to give a more accurate estimate of performance and to make sure that the
models were in fact evaluating the classification problem at hand (depressed vs. healthy controls),
in contrast to recognizing alternative patterns belonging to individual study participants (also
known as personal information leakage). Personal information leakage is a common finding in
depression analysis and leads to overfitting and higher classification performances [36].
Although this method was employed, validation on the post-treatment data provided with low
accuracies of approximately 61% based on the same features selected for the pre-dataset and
implied that it is diﬀicult to successfully build a generalized model for detection of depression.
The analysis framework proposed in this thesis may have also contributed to these findings, as
the selection of model parameters was conditional on the previously selected variables, introducing
model selection bias. For example, the analyses of optimal model parameters were based on other
previously selected model parameters presenting with high classification performances. This may
result in significantly overfitting the machine learning models and must be carefully considered
for future work.
However, it must be acknowledged that drawing a valid post-model-selection
conclusion is nearly impossible as investigating all combinations of variables is impractical and
ineﬀicient.
Clinical Applications. As the machine learning models of this thesis did not provide generalized
performances across multiple independent dataset and due to equally small sample sizes of the ma-
jority of related literature, it is still unknown whether generalized methods based on EEG signal
processing and machine learning exist for detection of depression. Based on this work, the variabil-
76
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:88==
ity in terms of methodologies proposed by state-of-the-art machine learning methods also greatly
affect the classification performances of the models and it is diﬀicult to accurately evaluate how
these have affected subsequent findings of publications. This variability constitutes a fundamental
problem and this, as well as the use of small datasets, model selection bias, overfitting due to data
leakage in cross-validation and lack of independent datasets may have presented unrealistically
high and biased performances. Therefore, to develop a classification model for implementation
into clinical settings, it is critical to assemble a dataset of increased size and diversity. This may
however be extremely diﬀicult and must consider the inevitable factors of EEG data acquisition
such as inconsistencies in the recording environment (use of equipment, room temperature, and
ambient noise), technician skills etc. which may affect signal variability. The inconsistencies of
such factors would contribute to higher inter-site data variability, which is required for making
robust models for implementation into the clinical environment. In this regard, a combination of
datasets must be assembled based on standardized data collection procedures and EEG acquisition
settings across multiple sites.
Data Acquisition. The primary dataset of investigation was provided for this thesis and included
high levels of noise and data of study participants acquired using different montages. Therefore,
the research questions of investigation were altered throughout the thesis and another dataset was
presented for reliability in contrast to most related work typically comprising of a single dataset. As
the second dataset consisted of 19 electrode channels, 19 electrode channels of Dataset A was used
throughout this thesis and in accordance with the majority of related work. This was important
as the high variability in terms of general methodologies used already make studies challenging to
compare as well as their robustness and ability to maintain performance with different variations of
data. Furthermore, it was also discovered that a couple of the data files of Dataset A were wrongly
labelled. These were therefore corrected prior to analysis to what was assumed to be correct. It is
however worth considering that some subjects may have been named incorrectly once more, and
that the data of some subjects may in fact belong to other subjects as eyes-closed or eyes-open
data.
EEG Montages. The datasets utilized in this thesis were both acquired based on the A1A2 linked-
ear referential standard electrode montage. This montage measures the electrode potentials at
individual electrode sites with reference to two linked electrodes located at the left (A1) and right
(A2) earlobes based on the assumption that these sites (the ears and mastoid bone) lack electrical
activity. According to Mumtaz et al. [16] however, the human body is not considered to have any
point which may be considered a perfect ground or at potential zero. Therefore, reference points
located on the human body such as the right and left ear may have impurities which might affect
analysis.
A comparative study of different EEG reference choices for diagnosing unipolar depression was
performed by Mumtaz et al. [16] and found that the choice of appropriate references is critical
during clinical decision-making as impure references could falsify measurements and subsequent
analysis results. The study hypothesized that a reference with zero potential such as the refer-
ence electrode standardization technique (REST) could improve the classification performance for
EEG analyses and when distinguishing between depressed and healthy controls. In this context,
classification was performed based on three different reference choices (linked ears (LE), average
reference (AR), and REST) and compared for evaluation purposes. The study reported significant
differences in the EEG-derived measures due to distinct EEG references and emphasizes the use of
REST when utilizing EEG data for clinical purposes such as performing classification of depressed
patients and healthy controls. The use of the REST reference, also known as the infinity reference
technique for EEG analysis has additionally been recommended in literature by Hu et al. [60] and
Lei et al. [61] and must be considered for future work.
8.2
Future Research Directions
This section presents further improvements of this project and possible matters of investigation.
Standardization. As proposed in this thesis and by related literature, standardization must be
performed to help generalize current methods for identification of depression (ie. the EEG montage,
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
77
==PAGE:89==
the number of electrodes, the sampling frequency, the artifact removal schemes etc.). This may
enable comparison between studies of interest and provide a standard for the implementation into
clinical settings.
Depression Analysis. For depression analysis, a wide range of psychometric questionnaires outlining
specific underlying criteria for depression are used to identify the severity of depression and may
not always correspond to each other or be an accurate measure of the depression state of the
brain. By establishing a clinical standard for the diagnosis of depression, a common ground would
be provided for clinicians and researchers for consistent and reliable diagnosis of depression and
would improve the generalization ability of automatic algorithms.
Data Availability. Future work must focus on providing more EEG datasets for depression. The
current unavailability of datasets sets certain limitations in terms of generalization ability of the
models and caution must therefore be adopted before translating the findings to clinical utilities.
In this regard, it may be beneficial to combine the datasets presented in this thesis and create
one combined model based on both eyes states from the pre-treatment datasets. The combined
post-treatment dataset for both eye states could then be used for validation of the model as an
independent dataset. This would provide a model which disregards the eye states and the variable
factors of EEG data acquisition.
In relation to combining datasets for analysis of depression,
studies must additionally consider the gender distribution and mean age of the study participants
to prevent signal contamination from gender- or age-related changes.
Artifact Removal Schemes. In this study, additional artifact removal schemes should be considered
as a supplement or alternative to ICA as it presents certain limitations and may demonstrate
unreliable results. ICA is additionally reliant on manual inspection of components in time and
frequency domains, as the order of the independent components cannot be determined and may
present with inconsistent findings dependent on the noise types and levels present for various
study participants. As presented by Cai et al. [2], a cascade of adaptive filters may be applied
as an automatic solution to reduce the common artifacts present in the signals without removing
significant information necessary for detection of depression.
Features. The robustness of the models may be further increased by introducing additional features
including functional connectivity measures such as synchronization likelihood between different
channels presented by Movahed et al [29] and further work must be done to evaluate previous
findings. Specifically, considering a combination of different types of EEG-derived features (ie.
wavelet-, temporal- and spectral features) must also be evaluated further and may provide with
significant findings for depression analysis.
Reproducability. As experienced throughout this thesis, studies rarely provide enough information
for reproducability and is a fundamental principle necessary to ensure that publications can be
trusted and to avoid misinformation. As a result, reproducability was considered in this work and
must likewise be considered for future work. In this regard, documenting the methods which do
not work is also of key importance and often overlooked by researchers.
Deep Learning. The concatenation of the conventional machine learning methods with deep learn-
ing solutions must also be considered, as deep learning architecture has the capability of extracting
features in automated ways and do not require the same level of human intervention. Further-
more, as proposed by Mahato et al. [32] additional work may focus on proposing machine learning
approaches for automatic severity scaling of depression.
78
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 9
Conclusions
As current diagnostic practice involves time-consuming subjective, inconsistent, and imprecise
clinical standards for diagnosis of clinical depression, the central focus of this thesis was establishing
automatic algorithms to maintain performance with different variations of data and help generalize
current state-of-the-art methods for automatic identification of depression. Such algorithms would
help provide a standard for the implementation into clinical settings as well as provide a fast, precise
and objective identification of depression, therapy eﬀicacy coupled with the pace of recovery or
treatment output. Therefore, independent datasets were implemented and analyzed in this thesis
through a series of state-of-the-art methods. The main results are summarized as follows.
Initially, it was found that the variability in terms of methodologies used for identification of
clinical depression greatly affect the performances of machine learning algorithms and should be
standardized. Specifically, the selection of classifiers, feature selection algorithms, classification
schemes, types of features, eye states of study participants, segmentation lengths and the number
of electrodes used for analysis were investigated and recognized to affect the final performances of
algorithms. Furthermore, the use of wavelet analysis in contrast to traditional methodologies were
investigated and suggest that both methods are useful for identification of depression.
According to the results provided in this thesis, the combination of the best performing model
parameters yields an average accuracy of 95.60%, sensitivity of 90.91%, specificity of 99.29%,
precision of 99.09% and F1-score of 94.81% using traditional linear features and paired inter-
hemispheric asymmetry measures from the central, temporal and occipital regions of the brain
in specific frequency bands. For evaluation of the overall methodology on another independent
dataset, using the same model parameters yielded an average accuracy of 86.67%, sensitivity of
85.91%, specificity of 87.50%, precision 88.16% and F1-score of 86.99%.
At last, yet another
dataset was used to validate the methodology. This time using only the top selected features of
the best performing model for training and classification. The results provided an average accuracy
of 61.90%, sensitivity of 25.00%, specificity of 84.62%, precision of 50.00% and F1-score of 33.33%.
Thus, not providing an acceptable and robust performance across datasets.
The results confirm that the proposed EEG feature sets provide high classification performances
but do not generalize well between datasets. Furthermore, it was found that outperforming state-
of-the-art approaches for identifying depression is achievable based on individual dataset, but
creating a robust model is more diﬀicult. Therefore, in accordance with related literature, more
data must be provided to accurately achieve such target and become independent to the varying
characteristics of EEG signals. At last, as this thesis is based on small sample sizes, the study
warrant a larger study to determine the clinical utility of the state-of-the-art methods applied
and caution must be adopted prior to translating findings presented throughout the thesis into
generalized depression behaviors.

Appendix A
Detailed Background
The objective of this chapter is to provide an in-depth description of some of the physiological
concepts presented in Section 2. In particular, this chapter will give an overview of the phases of
the action potential, the pyramidal neurons that form the building blocks of EEG, the mechanisms
of depression and common brain stimulation therapies other than tDCS available for treatment of
depression.
A.1
Phases of the Action Potential
The action potential may be divided into five phases: the rising phase, the peak phase, the falling
phase, the undershoot phase, and the refractory period, see Figure A.1.
Figure A.1: A schematic presentation of the nerve action potential [62].
A.1.1
Stimulation and rising phase
When a neuron is at rest, the interior of the cell is negatively charged relative to the exterior
of the cell. This voltage difference is called the resting membrane potential and is about −50
to −70 milivolts (mV). The value specifically depends on the types of open ion channels and
the concentrations of ions in the intracellular and extracellular fluids. Typically, there is a high
concentration of potassium (K+) ions in the intracellular fluid compared to the extracellular fluid,
while there is a high concentration of sodium (Na+) and chloride (Cl+) ions in the extracellular
fluid compared to the intracellular fluid. The concentration gradient (difference in concentrations)
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
87
==PAGE:99==
causes ions to move from higher to lower concentrations while electrostatic effects (attraction of
opposite charges) cause ions to move in and out of the neuron.
As the membrane is is more
permeable to K+, there is a constant efflux of K+ down its concentration gradient causing the
resting potential. This concentration gradient is also maintained by the sodium–potassium pump
(Na+/K+ ATPase) that continuously exports three Na+ ions and imports two K+ for every ATP
molecule. This causes a higher concentration of Na+ ions in the extracellular fluid, driving them
into the cell when the voltage-gated Na+ ion channels open during depolarization. If depolarization
is large enough, an inward current of Na+ ions will change the membrane potential from negative
to more positive. If the threshold potential is reached at −55mV , a positive feedback mechanism
will increase sodium permeability until the channels are fully open producing an action potential.
The sharp rise in sodium permeability and positive change in membrane potential correspond to
the rising phase of the action potential, which propagates as a wave along the axon by depolarizing
adjacent sections of the membrane.
A.1.2
Peak and falling phase
The peak phase of the action potential is caused as the membrane potential reaches sodium equi-
librium at +55mV , at which the sodium channels starts to close and causes the voltage-gated K+
ion channels to open. As a consequence, K+ ions now travel down the electrostatic gradient out
of the cell repolarizing the membrane by driving the voltage potential back towards the resting
membrane potential.
A.1.3
Afterhyperpolarization
Typically, repolarization of the membrane potential overshoots the resting membrane potential
and is caused by the continuous efflux of K+ ions through K+ channels or influx of Cl– through
Cl– channels during the action potential. This persists until the channels fully close restoring the
membrane permeability to its resting state.
A.1.4
Refractory period
The areas of the membrane which have recently been depolarized cannot depolarize again due to
the refractory period. The refractory period is divided into the absolute refractory period and
the relative refractory period. In the absolute refractory period, it is impossible to evoke another
action potential due to an inactive state of the voltage gated Na+ channels and in the relative
refractory period, a stronger stimulus is required for depolarization, as more Na+ channels recover.
The refractory periods make sure that the action potential only propagates in one direction, which
is essential for transmitting information across cells [14][63].
A.2
Pyramidal Neurons
Pyramidal neurons of the cortex are examples of neurons from where LFPs may be recorded using
an EEG device. This is because the neurons, apart from the condition of being synchronously acti-
vated, must form a functional entity and be geometrically aligned for the electric fields to be picked
up at a distance. The pyramidal neurons are spatially organized and aligned next to each other
with their main axes perpendicular to the cortical surface. When these neurons experience a syn-
chronized input from postsynaptic potentials, intracellular and extracellular currents flow along the
dendritic tree to the cell body (soma) creating a strong dipole (adjacent areas of opposite charge).
The longitudinal components of these currents add while their transverse components cancel. The
result is an open field, which may be recorded at the scalp surface. Simultaneously, local magnetic
fields (LMFs) are also generated forming the building blocks of magnetoencephalography (MEG),
which measures brain activity by recording the magnetic fields produced by neuronal electrical
currents. The pyramidal neurons of the cortex are thought to produce the majority of EEG and
MEG signals as they are spatially aligned and fire in a coordinated manner. As the strength of
field gradients fall with the square of distance, it is more diﬀicult to detect activity from deeper
sources [11][64].
88
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:100==
Figure A.2: The neurophysiological foundation of EEG. a) EEG signals reflect activity from syn-
chronously activated pyramidal neurons in the cortex via extracellular currents known as excitatory
postsynaptic potentials (EPSPs) which create dipoles perpendicular to the surface. b) The neurons
are mediated at the synapses by arrival of action potentials and the release of neurotransmitters
(NT) which trigger and EPSP. c) The cortex is organized in six layers (I-IV) and the pyramidal
neurons are typically located in layers III and V. These neurons generate the majority of EEG
signals, as they are spatially aligned and perpendicular to the cortical surface. EEG recorded
on the scalp surface represent a summation of postsynaptic potentials from numerous of neurons
near each electrode. d) The postsynaptic potentials cross several layers of tissues with different
electrical properties and geometry. e) Electrodes are typically positioned on the scalp according
to international standards enabling studies to be compared. [65].
A.3
Mechanisms of Depression
A combination of factors may be essential for understanding the precise mechanisms by which
depression develops as well as the pathophysiology of depression. These factors have been explained
in detail below.
A.3.1
The monoamine hypothesis
The monoamine hypothesis proposes that a depletion in the levels of monoamine neurotransmitters
(serotonin, norepinephrine, and dopamine) results in decreased neural transmission and impaired
cognitive performance leading to depression. Neurotransmitters are signaling molecules released
from synaptic vesicles of neurons into synaptic clefts where they interact with neurotransmitter
receptors on target cells. Norepinephrine exerts its effects on many areas of the brain such as the
prefrontal cortex, processing of the working memory as well as behavior and attention. Serotonin
innervates the whole central nervous system and is essential for regulating mood, affection, cog-
nition, aggression, satiety, sleep as well as numerous other autonomic functions, while dopamine
is important for brain functions such as voluntary movement and a broad array of behavioral
processes including mood, reward, addiction, working memory, attention and stress [66].
These signaling molecules are essential for the function of neural systems and have been found
to contribute to many behavioural manifestations of depression such as changes in mood, ap-
petite, motivation, concentration, aggression and fatigue. The decreased neural transmission of
monoamines essential for the hypothesis may also result from decreased protein transporter func-
tions or abnormalities in the neurotransmitter receptors. Other neurotransmitters such as gluta-
mate and gamma-aminobutyric acid (GABA) have also been linked to depression [19].
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
89
==PAGE:101==
A.3.2
Genetic factors
Studies of clinical depression within families and twins provide strong evidence for a genetic con-
tribution to the risk of depression. A meta-analysis of five twin studies conducted by Sullivan et
al. [67] suggested that heritability of MDD is likely to be in the range of 31-42%, but adds that
it might be substantially higher for reliably diagnosed MDD or depression subtypes. The meta-
analysis also considered five family studies and revealed that first-degree relatives have a two-
to threefold increase in risk of developing depression. A number of genes have specifically been
associated with depression and found to bring about significant differences in terms of regional
anatomical differences of the anterior cingulate cortex and prefrontal cortex [68].
A.3.3
Environmental factors
Environmental factors of depression may be present in the form of air, water and food pollution or
may include social and relational events like childhood abuse, prolonged stress, loss of a loved one,
or traumatic events. It may be synthetic chemicals in the form of food additives and preservatives,
pesticides, hormones and drugs, and industrial byproducts, or non-chemical contributions such as
noise pollution, electrical pollution, natural disasters, and other catastrophic environmental events
[69]. The influence of such factors and adverse life events is well documented and the majority of
researchers report severe environmental stress before the onset of depression [19]. According to
several studies, various forms of stress may induce structural and functional changes in the brain
by disrupting synapse regulation and reducing the size of the prefrontal cortex, contributing to
both the emotional and cognitive impairment evident in depression [70][71][72].
A.3.4
Endocrine factors
The endocrine system regulates critical physiological processes by releasing and monitoring hor-
mones important for metabolism, growth and development, emotions and mood, fertility and sexual
function, sleep and blood pressure. Specifically, altered levels of growth hormone (GH), abnor-
malities of thyroid hormone levels and dysfunction of the Hypothalamus-Pituitary-Adrenal (HPA)
axis have all been identified as contributors to the etiology of depression. In literature, however,
the hyperactivity of the HPA axis has been the most frequently occurring endocrine abnormality
and is targeted by many antidepressants. The hyperactivity of the HPA axis disrupts complex
mechanisms of normal functioning by contributing to an excess release of cortisol from the adrenal
glands. The hyper-secretion of cortisol along with dysfunctional glucocorticoid feedback mecha-
nisms and inadequate HPA axis suppression then leads to a desensitization of cortisol receptors,
which in turn leads to increased activity of pro-inflammatory immune mediators and disturbances
in neurotransmitter transmission associated with the intensity and severity of depression [19][73].
A.3.5
Immunologic factors: the role of cytokines
The pathogenesis of depression is often associated with biomarkers indicative of immunologic or
inflammatory cytokines. These include elevated levels of interleukins IL-1￿, IL-2, IL-4, IL-6, IL-
8, IL-10 and interferon gamma (IFN-γ), c-reactive protein (CRP), tumor necrosis factor alpha
(TNF￿), and the chemokine monocyte chemoattractant protein-1 (MCP-1/CCL2). As presented by
Strawbridge et al. [74], research evidence suggests that abnormal inflammatory responses appear in
approximately one-third of patients with depression and play a significant role in its development.
A number of direct and indirect links have been proposed with various studies linking chronic
stress (as a factor of depression) with impaired functions of the immune system [19].
A.3.6
Neuroinflammation
Several inflammatory illnesses of the brain including systemic lupus erythematosus, traumatic brain
injury and multiple sclerosis are associated with a high prevalence of depression. The majority
of research evidence also suggests that neuroinflammatory changes or responses in specific areas
of the brain overlap with the symptoms of depression, specifically determined by an increase of
translocator protein density measured by distribution volume (TSPO VT) in the prefrontal cortex,
anterior cingulate cortex and insula [19].
90
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:102==
A.3.7
Neurogenesis
Neurogenesis in adults is the process by which functional neurons and neuronal connections are
formed in the dentate gyrus of the hippocampus and the sub-ventricular area of the lateral ven-
tricles. More recently, however, research has confirmed that neurogenesis is also evident in the
hypothalamus, striatum, substantia nigra, cortex, and amygdala [75]. With regard to depression
pathophysiology, research has proposed that alterations in neurogenesis capacity causes depression.
However, conflicting results exists as several antidepressants are independent of neurogenesis and
the absence of neurogenesis in experimental animals does not always lead to depressive symptoms.
Therefore, it seems most likely that the alterations of neurogenesis capacity, which often accom-
pany the behavioral changes of depression, is just one, more visible aspect of occurring neuroplastic
phenomena and that an array of complex factors are involved in the pathophysiology of depression
[76].
A.3.8
Linked mechanisms of depression
As the monoamine hypothesis, neuroinflammation, neurogenesis as well as the genetic, environ-
mental, immunologic, and endocrine factors are unable to singly explain the pathogenesis of depres-
sion, depression remains a disorder of heterogenous nature in which an interplay of complex factors
contribute to its development. When analyzing the plausible connections between each of the con-
tributing factors however, the HPA axis provides a central link, described in the ”Diasthesis-Stress
hypothesis”. The hypothesis highlights that environmental stressors acting through an immuno-
logic response and heritable genetic factors may initiate structural and functional of the brain
resulting in alterations in neurogenesis capacity and neurotransmission resulting in decreased neu-
ral transmission and impaired cognitive performance leading to the combination of symptoms seen
in depression [19]. Thus, a combination of these factors may be essential for understanding the
precise mechanisms by which depression develops as well as the pathophysiology of depression.
A.4
Brain Stimulation Therapies
A brief description of the procedure and effect of brain stimulation therapies including electrocon-
vulsive therapy, transcranial magnetic stimulation and vagus nerve stimulation is described in the
sections below for comparison to tDCS therapy investigated in this thesis.
A.4.1
Electroconvulsive Therapy (ECT)
Electroconvulsive therapy (ECT), also known as electroshock, is typically used on individuals
with treatment-resistant and/or severe depression. The procedure is done by placing the subject
under general anesthesia and inducing small electric currents through the brain triggering a brief
seizure. The seizure increases the activity of specific serotonergic and noradrenergic pathways in
the brain and has been found to increase neurogenesis. The effect of ECT has been under intense
investigation and studies have found that approximately 80% of individuals affected by a severe
depression gets better after ECT. Side effects may include confusion, headache or muscle pain, as
well as problems with remembering things before or after treatment and a diﬀiculty of learning
new things. In addition, the relapse rate of depression has been reported as high as 84% within the
first 6 months of ECT and remains a major challenge for clinicians as well as affected individuals
[77][78].
A.4.2
Transcranial Magnetic Stimulation (TMS)
Transcranial magnetic stimulation (TMS), also known as repetitive TMS or rTMS, is typically used
in cases where antidepressant medication is unsuccessful. The stimulation procedure is done by
placing an electromagnetic coil near the prefrontal cortex and creating a magnetic field. Repetitive
magnetic pulses are then used to cause an inductance of inverted electrical charge within the
brain to ease the symptoms of depression. However, the antidepressant effects of TMS and the
neurophysiological mechanisms involved are not completely understood. Adverse effects are rare
but common side effects typically include headache, scalp discomfort, tingling, spasms or twitching
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
91
==PAGE:103==
of facial muscles and lightheadedness. Patients may relapse weeks or months after acute TMS
treatment [79][80].
A.4.3
Vagus nerve stimulation (VNS)
Vagus nerve stimulation (VNS) is another type of brain stimulation therapy that traditionally
includes surgery of a pacemaker-like device into the chest near the collarbone and a stimulating
wire to the vagus nerve. This is usually done for people whose depression have resisted other treat-
ments. However, new non-invasive vagus nerve stimulation devices have recently been approved in
Europe to treat pain, epilepsy and depression. Studies suggest that it may take up to 6 months or
even a year before symptoms of depression start to fade. Side effects may include voice changes,
hoarseness, throat pain, cough, headaches, shortness of breath, diﬀiculty swallowing, tingling or
prickling of the skin, insomnia or worsening of sleep apnea [81].
92
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:104==

Appendix C
Exploratory Analysis
This chapter presents a brief exploratory data analysis using electrode channels and established
features considered in prior art for automatic detection of depression using EEG signals. Specifi-
cally, this chapter investigates well-known EEG features of literature as well as features based on
a correlation metric which may reveal features and electrode channels of particular importance for
identification of depression.
Investigating non-linear trends and mixed trends across temporal-, spectral- and wavelet analysis
is not included this section but is considered by advanced feature selection schemes and classifiers
in the remaining chapters of this thesis. The relationships presented in this chapter are only meant
to show the degree of information that is more easily accessible and investigate if simple trends
exist for automatic identification of depression.
C.1
Exploration of Electrodes using Correlation
To search for pairs of electrode channels that correlate differently for depressed and healthy individ-
uals, an exploratory correlation analysis was performed using a normalized correlation difference
metric (M) presented in (C.1).
M = |µρ,D −µρ,H|
σρ,D + σρ,H
,
(C.1)
where µ and σ are the mean and standard deviation of the correlation (ρ) between pairs of EEG
channels for depressed (D) and healthy (H) controls, respectively.
The difference metric was
constructed to provide a measure of the difference between the respective groups in relation to
the dispersion of the samples and the results of analysis using the difference metric are presented
in Figure C.1 and C.2 for Dataset A and B, respectively. The analysis is based on 120 s of both
datasets in the eyes-open and eyes-closed state. The matrices presenting the M value for each
pair of electrode channels in Figure C.1 suggest that some electrodes elicit a differential response
in depressed and healthy controls and that the importance of the electrodes differs between eyes-
closed and eyes-open state. For example, for the eyes-closed state presented in Figure C.1a, the
electrode pairs TP8-A2 vs FCz-A1 and TP8-A2 vs Fz-A1 show large differences between the normal
and depressed class with the normalized correlation metric above 0.6.
While in the eyes-open
state presented in Figure C.1b, the results indicate that the asymmetric frontal pairs Fp1-A1 vs
F4-A2 and Fp2-A2 vs F3-A1 have different activity across the classes. These findings may indicate
important channels of future analyses but it is important to acknowledge that the participant
samples are limited in number and may change according to the dataset of investigation. Therefore,
the exploratory data analysis was extended to Dataset B, with corresponding M values for each
pair of electrodes presented in Figure C.2.
With regard to the difference correlation matrices presented in Figure C.2, the channel pairs T4-A2
vs Fz-A1 and P4-A2 vs Cz-A2 seem particularly important for distinguishing between the depressed
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
97
==PAGE:109==
and healthy controls and present a correlation difference metric approximately equivalent to 0.8 in
both the eyes-open and eyes-closed state. By considering the correlation results of the electrodes
presented in Figure C.1 and C.2, it seems that different electrodes may play significant roles in
differentiating between depressed and healthy controls and that the importance of electrodes may
depend on the state of the eyes during data acquisition. The results also indicate that the frontal
and/or temporal electrodes may play a significant role in distinguishing between the groups, as
suggested by prior art including Cai et al. [2], Radenkovic et al. [3], Mahato et al. [32] and
Mumtaz et al. [44].
An investigative plot of selected correlation pairs of Dataset A in the eyes-closed state is presented
in Figure C.3. In Figure C.3, it is clear that the correlation metrics have high distinguishable
characteristics. The data points from depressed individuals generally lie in the upper right part of
the plot, ie. positive correlation for both channel pairs, while data points for the healthy patients
lie in the lower left part of the plot, i.e. low or slightly negative correlation for both channel pairs.

C.2
Exploration of Features
A preliminary exploratory data analysis of temporal and spectral features was conducted based
on the same principle of (C.1). This was done to investigate if simple trends exist for automatic
detection of depression and to refine features for extraction and subsequent binary classification.
The exploratory analysis of this section is limited to the participants of Dataset A in the eyes-closed
state and may therefore vary across different datasets. Like the previous section, the analysis was
conducted based on the maximum length of the signals provided.
C.2.1
Temporal Features
Temporal features of this investigation include the linear features of Table 6.1 presented in Chapter
6.2. The features were investigated across all the electrode channels based on the following feature
importance metric (F) given by:
F = |µD −µH|
σD + σH
,
(C.2)
where µ and σ are the mean and standard deviation of the features for respective EEG channels
for depressed (D) and healthy (H) controls, respectively. Examples of the top performing temporal
features according to the normalized difference metric are presented in Figure C.4a and C.4b. In
Figure C.4a and C.4b, it seems that simple linear features perform well in distinguishing between
depressed and healthy subjects.
C.2.2
Spectral Features
Spectral features presented in this exploratory analysis includes band power features from the delta
(0.5-4 Hz), theta (4-8 Hz), alpha (8-16 Hz), beta (16-32 Hz), gamma (32-70 Hz) and all (0.5-70
Hz) frequency bands. The power in the frequency bands was estimated using Welch’s method
described in Section 6.2.1.2 using a Hanning window of 2 s and 50% overlap between segments.
The spectral power features were similarly to the temporal features investigated across all electrode
channels based on the normalized feature importance metric, F, presented in (C.2). Examples of the
linear relationship between some of the top spectral features in distinguishing between depressed
and healthy controls according to the equation are presented in Figure C.4c and Figure C.4d.
The figures indicate that spectral features, specifically within the alpha band may possess good
predictive power in aiding differential diagnosis of depression as proposed by related literature.
Specifically, inter-hemispheric asymmetry of band power in the alpha band has been of great in-
terest in automatic detection of depression and highlighted in previous literature such as Acharya
et al. [34], Mumtaz et al. [44], Schaffer et al. [82], Gollan et al. [83] and Bruder et al. [84]. There-
fore, the absolute power of electrode channels per frequency band is investigated and presented
in Figure C.5 for the complete brain and the left and right hemispheres for comparison between
depressed and healthy controls.
In Figure C.5, the prominent frequencies in the eyes-closed state lie within the delta and alpha
frequency bands. From the values, it can be observed that there is only little power in the gamma
frequency band. With respect to the importance of inter-hemispheric asymmetry in distinguishing
between classes, the absolute power within the whole brain and left and right hemispheres may
not be particularly influential. As described by related literature however, other conclusions may
be revealed by further investigating asymmetry within specific brain regions such as the frontal or
temporal regions.
In Figure C.6, the absolute power in individual electrode channels within the delta and alpha
frequency bands are investigated. The figure shows that particular electrode channels like T6-A2
and TP8-A2 may prove important for identification of depression as the mean of the absolute power
and corresponding standard deviation vary slightly more between both groups compared to the
other channels. As previously indicated however, it is important to acknowledge that differences
102
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:114==
may occur across datasets due to the limited number of participants present in the study (total of
25 in the pre-treatment case).
Figure C.5: Absolute power of EEG channels per frequency band presented for the whole brain
and the left and right hemispheres among depressed and healthy control subjects.

Appendix D
Algorithm Description
This chapter presents the frequency response and filter characteristics of the filters applied in this
thesis and an example of ICA components investigated for removal of pulse and eye-blink artifacts.
104
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
==PAGE:116==
D.1
Filter Characteristics
This section presents the frequency response and filter characteristics of the bandstop and bandpass
filters presented in this thesis to suppress artifacts of fixed frequencies.
(a) Bandstop filter. Filter characteristics: Chebyshev type II in sos format, stopband
edge frequencies: 49 and 51 Hz, passband edge frequencies: 48 and 52 Hz, maximum
loss in the passband: 3 dB, minimum attenuation in stopband: 60 dB, filter order: 6.
(b) Bandpass filter. Filter characteristics: Chebyshev type II in sos format, stopband
edge frequencies: 0.4 and 70 Hz, passband edge frequencies: 0.5 and 69 Hz, maximum
loss in the passband: 3 dB, minimum attenuation in stopband: 60 dB, filter order: 6.
Figure D.1: The frequency response and characteristics of the bandstop and bandpass filters applied
to the EEG signals. Data used: S4 (depressed) with eyes-closed prior to treatment with tDCS.
