Appendix E
Outcomes
This chapter presents analysis outcomes of this thesis related to brain asymmetry, investigation of
classifiers, eye states, segmentation lengths in the frontal region and wavelet analysis outcomes.
E.1
Brain Asymmetry Analysis Outcomes
This section presents the results of computing the inter-hemispheric asymmetry and paired inter-
hemispheric asymmetry between depressed and healthy groups in the eyes-open and pre-treatment
case for Dataset A. The particular electrode channels used for computing the measures are specified
in Table 6.5 in Section 6.2.1.3. In Figure E.1 and E.2, it can be seen that large standard deviations
are present for both classes, and that the classes may be hard to differentiate in most frequency
bands.
In Figure E.1, it is seen that inter-hemispheric asymmetry may differentiate between healthy and
depressed classed in the delta and gamma frequency band. In Figure E.2, for the healthy controls,
the asymmetry of the temporal region seems to be higher in most frequency bands and reflects
increased activity of the right hemisphere compared to depressed subjects.
Figure E.1: Inter-hemispheric asymmetry of Dataset A in the eyes-open state. The figure shows
the average log-ratio of left and right hemispheric power grouped by frequency bands, including
all, delta, theta, alpha, beta and gamma bands, respectively. The uncertainty bar represents a
single standard deviation. Positive values represent increased activity of the right hemisphere and
negative values represent increased activity of the left hemisphere. The findings suggest that inter-
hemispheric asymmetry may differentiate between healthy and depressed classed in the delta and
gamma frequency band. The high standard deviation of the classes indicate that the variation is
substantial within the samples.
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
107
==PAGE:119==
Figure E.2: Paired inter-hemispheric asymmetry of depressed and healthy controls of Dataset A
in the eyes-open and pre-treatment case. The figure shows the average log-ratio of left and right
hemispheric power in six frequency bands, including all, delta, theta, alpha, beta and gamma
bands, respectively. For every band, the asymmetry is visualized according to six brain regions,
including the pre-frontal, frontal, central, parietal, temporal and occipital regions. The uncertainty
bar represents a single standard deviation. Positive values represent increased activity of the right
hemisphere and negative values represent increased activity of the left hemisphere. The findings
suggest that inter-hemispheric asymmetry in the temporal region is higher in healthy controls in
all bands except the gamma frequency band.

Appendix F
Python Code Developed
This chapter presents the Python code developed for this thesis. The code is structured into three
overall stages including:
1. Importing and pre-processing of Dataset A and B.
2. Segmentation and feature extraction.
3. Feature selection and classification.
Data files are constructed and saved per subject and for each script to allow working in various
stages of the framework. In terms of the names thesis_code and eeg_data_new, these refer to
Dataset A and B, respectively.
F.1
Import and Pre-processing
This section presents the Python code developed for importing and pre-processing of Dataset A
and B. This includes filtering the EEG signals with bandpass and bandstop filters, downsampling
from 500 to 256 Hz and ICA artifact removal. The code is organized in two main scripts for Dataset
A and B and calls functions developed in additional scripts presented as follows.
F.1.1
Dataset A: Main Script
1

Filter Functions

from scipy import signal
2
3
# bandstop filter
4
def bandstop_filter(raw,fs):
5
# set parameters
6
lower_pass = 48
7
lower_stop = 49
8
upper_pass = 52
9
upper_stop = 51
10
11
ws = [lower_stop/(fs*0.5), upper_stop/(fs*0.5)] # stopband edge frequencies
12
wp = [lower_pass/(fs*0.5), upper_pass/(fs*0.5)] # passband edge frequencies
13
rp = 3 # maximum loss in pb
14
rs = 60 # minimum attenuation in sb
15
16
# calculate filter order and cut-off frequency
17
N, Wn = signal.cheb2ord(wp,ws,rp,rs)
18
# print('Order of the filter=', N)
19
# print('Cut-off frequency=', Wn)
20
21
# second-order sections (sos) implementation is required to ensure stability of the
filter
22
sos_bs = signal.cheby2(N, rs, Wn, btype='bandstop', output='sos')
23
24
# Frequency response
25
w, h = signal.sosfreqz(sos_bs, worN=2000,fs=fs)
26
27
# Apply forward-backward filter using cascaded second-order sections
Detection of Major Depressive Disorder (MDD)
for Diagnosis and Treatment Response
121
==PAGE:133==
28
# This function applies a linear digital filter twice, once forward and once
backwards.
29
# The combined filter has zero phase and a filter order twice that of the original.
30
filtered_bs = signal.sosfiltfilt(sos_bs, raw, axis=-1,padtype='odd')
31
32
return filtered_bs , N, Wn
33
34
# bandpass filter
35
def bandpass_filter(raw,segment,fs,bp_edge):
36
lower_stop , upper_stop , lower_pass, upper_pass = bp_edge
37
# Set parameters
38
ws = [lower_stop/(fs*0.5), upper_stop/(fs*0.5)] # stopband edge frequencies
39
wp = [lower_pass/(fs*0.5), upper_pass/(fs*0.5)] # passband edge frequencies
40
rp = 3 # maximum loss in pb
41
rs = 60 # minimum attenuation in sb
42
43
# Calculate filter order and cut-off frequency
44
N, Wn = signal.cheb2ord(wp,ws,rp,rs)
45
#print('Filter order =', N)
46
#print('Cut-off frequency =', Wn)
47
48
# Second-order sections (sos) implementation is required to ensure stability of the
filter
49
sos_bp = signal.cheby2(N, rs, Wn, btype='bandpass', output='sos')
50
51
# Frequency response
52
w, h = signal.sosfreqz(sos_bp, worN=2000,fs=fs)
53
54
# Apply forward-backward filter using cascaded second-order sections
55
# This function applies a linear digital filter twice, once forward and once
backwards.
56
# The combined filter has zero phase and a filter order twice that of the original.
57
filtered_bp = signal.sosfiltfilt(sos_bp, segment, axis=-1, padtype='odd')
58
59
return filtered_bp , N, Wn

# resample data using linear interpolation
66
def resample_linear(original, targetLen):
67
original = np.array(original, dtype=np.float) # filtered signal
68
index_arr = np.linspace(0, len(original)-1, num=targetLen, dtype=np.float)
69
index_floor = np.array(index_arr, dtype=np.int) # round down
70
index_ceil = index_floor + 1
71
index_rem = index_arr - index_floor
72
73
val1 = original[index_floor]
74
val2 = original[index_ceil % len(original)]
75
interp = val1 * (1.0-index_rem) + val2 * index_rem
76
assert(len(interp) == targetLen)
77
return interp

import numpy as np

import pywt

4
# wavelet decomposition and reconstruction of wavelet coefficients
5
def wave_sig(segment, level, wave):
6
7
wavelet_sig = []
8
wave_list = list(range(level+1))
9
for i in wave_list:
10
11
# calculate wavelet coefficients
12
coeffs = pywt.wavedec(segment, wave, mode='symmetric', level=level, axis=-1)
13
14
# coefficients to remove
15
wave_remove = wave_list[:i] + wave_list[i+1:]
16
# remove approximation (cA) or detail coefficients (cD)
17
for j in wave_remove:
18
coeffs[-j] = np.zeros_like(coeffs[-j])
19
20
# recontruct signal using the coeffs of the DWT decomposition
21
wavelet = pywt.waverec(coeffs, wave, axis=-1)
22
wavelet_sig.append(wavelet)
23
24
return wavelet_sig
25
26
# wavelet decomposition into wavelet coefficients
27
def wave_coef(segment,level,wave):
28
29
# calculate wavelet coefficients
30
coeffs = pywt.wavedec(segment, wave, mode='symmetric', level=level, axis=-1)
31
#[cA6, cD6, cD5, cD4, cD3, cD2, cD1] = coeffs
32
33
return coeffs

def KFold(f_sel_method ,df_master,k,GLOB_PATTERN ,n_features ,N_NEIGHBORS):
26
27
# split feature matrix into depressed and healthy
28

30

31

32
33

34

35

36

37
